RATING: 5
1-5 plays. I was extremely disappointed with this as I was convinced I would really like it. It oozes theme and I think the Fellowship/Ring to Mordor/Hunt mechanism is excellent. However, at its heart, it's a wargame - and a distinctly average one at that (if anything it reminded me of Axis & Allies but not as slick). The game is overly long with much of the time spent fiddling with the pieces and understanding the plethora of rules nuances and the exceptions created by the cards. Whilst the board and pieces look extremely attractive, in game terms, the implementation is poor; Mountains and regional borders are not particularly clear; units just don't fit in the regions and regularly cover key cities/strongholds which, if you're not totally familiar with the map, can be fatal; the decision to (presumably) cut costs by making allied nations all the same colour makes it virtually impossible to distinguish them resulting in a distinct loss of gameplay and a feeling that you spend more time trying to understand where you are rather than actually playing the game. Now I accept that this is based on one play and familiarity will address some of these issues but I found Squad Leader easier to take in and less clunky after one outing and the prospect of spending another 20 or 30 hours just to get to a level where I can start to think about enjoying it as a game is not my idea of fun. I'd be happy to play again but I would much prefer it if some of the theme had been sacrificed for playability and a shorter playing time. And a final note, to flag this as 2-4 player game is merely to satisfy the marketing people. This is a 2 player game. The rules for 3 & 4 are tagged on at the end in an attempt to sell more copies.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.6
Awesome game. Excellent bits and board, very immersive. It is probably my favorite 2-player game, and it is THE definitive Lord of the Rings game. If you want an epic Lord of the Rings gaming experience, this game cannot be beat.

During the game, both players must allocate their actions between the fellowship of the ring (either finding them or getting them to Mordor) and the War between the Free Peoples and the Shadow. Each nation has their own miniatures and the cards do a very good job of reenacting situations (or possible situations) from the books. The action dice are a cool part of the game that can force you to alter your strategy a bit, but still gives you a ton of choices.

I love the full game, but also enjoy a variant on the intro game I made that cuts out the cards (thus, cutting play time in half) and is more balanced than the original (broken) quick-play rules.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
From a design standpoint, it's simply breathtaking how faithful this is to the War of the Ring story.  Any one who's looked at the fiction of the War of the Ring can appreciate just how non-linear it is -- in fact, this is what made me eventually realize just how more universal the Ring trilogy is than Star Wars could ever hope to be.  

Everything is here, the brutal grind of the armies flung at one another juxtaposed with the vital quest to render the ring.  And the game itself, in the 2nd edition is just fantastically, lovingly produced;  you can simply feel the positive energy that went into its creation knowing the design itself was already proven and successful.

Still, I have to say this game didn't entirely work for us, and least not in the sense that we want to play it alot.  This is a bleep-we're-snowed-in-for-36-hours kind of game -- not the kind of game you want to impulsively play again immediately after finishing.  The quest of the ring is exhausting for all, the stakes so high, that it makes no sense to trifle with it strictly for diversion. 

This is a game you must be prepared to live....
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The LotR theme is very well woven into this game, resembling very much the actual situation known from the books. The components are beautifully made and if you like detailed plastic miniatures, then you'll drool happily over what you get here. The mechanics works well, you draw cards (random factor), roll dice (random factor) and then decide what you do based on what you get, which makes strategic planning and choices possible. Combat boils down to a simple dice rolling mechanic. This is the type of game where a good strategy will win the game only occasionally. There is unfortunately too much 'luck' involved for me to find it really awesome.

RATING: 9
This game does a great job of capturing the theme and spirit of the story while not going down the slippery slope of being scripted. All of the elements you'd expect to be in the game are there and then some but you are free to rewrite 'history' or follow the same path. There are so many options available to both players that I doubt the game will play out the same way twice. Sure, there are several seemingly sure fire overall strategies that are mentioned a lot but even if both players try the same strategy twice they will always be different since the cards are such a significant part of how the game unfolds. Lots of choices and tension all the way through to the end. The corruption buildup at the end of the game seems a little harsh on the Fellowship but I guess that's really the way it should be otherwise it would seem too easy! Overall, a solid game that I'd play almost anytime if I had the time.

RATING: 8
Bring elements of card-driven wargames (the cards themselves and the action dice that have a similar limiting effect on options) to a simple buckets o' dice combat system. Add a cleverly abstracted sub-game in which the Fellowship treks towards Mordor, dogged by Sauron's minions and picking up corruption. Add a varied set of characters and interesting decisions over how to use them best.

The graphic design seems to place looks over function; you will frequently be squinting at names and borders, losing important details under masses of plastic pieces, struggling to remember the meaning of pretty, but opaque icons.  Those plastic pieces are also very good looking, but less than functional.  The different Free peoples are too hard to tell apart.  I found the rules a bit verbose, but not nearly as messy as I'd been led to expect.

Despite those minor criticisms - and not having read the books or seen the movies - I found War of the Ring a highly immersive experience.  The various elements all come together brilliantly.

RATING: 6
Besides to big plastics - what's wrong with counters - for to small areas, the whole game doesn't feel like a solid whole. Didn't they know the card driven games. It could have been a great source of inspiration and proper game design. The cardtexts are often too long and too complicated. There is no timeline and cards appear random. The decks are too large and I do not see the usefullness of the double card deck.

Most of the action takes place in Rohan and Gondor, since it is very time consuming to move armies large distances. So I was wondering why there so many miniatures at all. And why doesn't the game start in The Shire as in the book.

Not an unenjoyable game, but clearly overhyped with an odd game design philosophy, if there was any.

RATING: 3
Unnecessarily verbose rules. Colossal amount of time to set up, made virtually impossible if you're not intimately familiar with middle earth's geography (even with the setup guides it takes a while, and not every nation referred to in the cards is in the lookup list in the rulebook). Gameplay mechanics are interesting -- variable action selection and variable hunt mechanism are quite clever and novel. Completely indistinguishable free people characters (yes, I know there are differences, no, I can't see them from three feet away), a horribly designed map (looks nice, difficult to read and play, not big enough for pieces, army replacement counters a hassle), and a preposterously verbose rulebook (which makes quick rules checks a lengthy process) make for an overall negative gaming experience for me. Not particularly interested in playing this one, not sure what all the fuss is about.

RATING: 8
I love how this game oozes with the LotR theme. I love the minis, the cards, and the game art. Frankly, everything component-wise is top notch.

The rulebook is a little crappy for such a relatively simple game - if the game isn't just explained to you, it can be quite daunting to learn.

The gameplay itself is VERY fun, but in all honesty is just a bit too luck based for my liking. The end of the game is basically dictated by whether or not you draw an eye tile - and when that happens the fellowship takes a pretty massive hit of corruption. This has caused the free peoples to lose in both games I've played. [[edit: I'm told that this is because I'm not stacking my hunt tiles properly. I'll try weighting it a bit more.]]

I do absolutely love the idea of the free peoples being limited in the number of components provided, while the shadow armies are unlimited. That adds an interesting dynamic.

RATING: 8
Még egyetlen játékra sem készültem ennyit, mint a Gyűrűk háborújára. Hetekig tartott, mire magyarítva elkészültek a kártyák, és a szabályt is elég nehéz volt összeszedni, az eredeti nagyon kusza. Szerintem a hagyományos módon (egyszer elolvasom a szabályt, és utána azonnal mehet a játék) teljesen esélytelen kipróblni. Több hetes rákészülés után végre tegnap kipróbáltuk a két személyes változatot, és felemás a tapasztalatom. Egyfelől nagyon egyedi a játékmenet, tetszik, ahogy megoldották a gyűrűhordozók bujkálását, és a romlottságukat. Tetszik az is, hogy mindkét fél 2 teljesen eltérő módon is nyerhet(katonailag, és a gyűrűn keresztül). De ahogy a rákészülés, úgy maga a játék is baromi időigényes. A felpakolás így kezdőként 1 óra volt, de egy haladónak is legalább fél óra lehet. Ha új emberrel játsszuk, a szabály ismertetés alsó hangon 1 óra. És maga a játék... nos a hivatalos 3 órával szemben, nagyon nehezen tudom elképzelni, hogy 5-6 órán belül vége legyen. Néhány órán belül odáig sem jut el a játék, hogy a nemzetek többsége hadba lépjen. Egy szó, mint száz, nagyon jó játék, de elrontja az élményt az, hogy külön életmódot igényel a rákészülés, és szabadságot kell kivenni, ha játszani akarunk vele. És még ezzel együtt is szeretnék újra játszani vele:)

Fontos még, hogy nem pont ugyanaz a katonák elosztása, mint a leírásban. Az össz darabszám stimmel, de jópár nemzet seregében +-1 katona van.
A másik, hogy a Szabad Népek játékos dobókockáin 2 oldal ugyanaz, pedig a leírás szerint 6 különböző oldala van. De minden akcióra lehetőség van így is, csak más eséllyel. A mi játékunkban pl. ezáltal felértékelődött a Gyűrűhordozók menekülésének lehetősége. Azért ez elég durva hiba, ha teljes szériában kiment így. De nyomozok még, hátha én értettem valamit félre:)
---
A második játék már jobban sikerült, és rövidebb is volt jóval: 3 órás partit játszottunk. A mostani tapasztalat az, hogy a Szabad Népek játékos szinte esélytelen. De még próbálkozunk majd:) Talán katonailag nyerhet az a fél is.
---
5 év elteltével újra játszottam, immár a 2. kiadással, és el kell ismernem, hogy nagyon egyedi és hangulatilag nagyon erős a játék. Visszamenőleg megbocsátom ennek az 1.0 verziónak is azt a néhány apró hibáját, és felpontozom. Vicces lenne, ha 2 pont különbséggel pontoznám az egyébként szinte tökéletesen megegyező játékokat.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
THE GOOD
I really love this game because of the event cards and the action dice. They are different every time, and though there is a finite number of cards, there is an almost limitless number of ways in which they will be drawn. It is almost as if each game is a rewriting of the book, chapter by chapter, paragraph by paragraph.

The balance of military action versus character maneuver also makes this game great. Players must always be ready to change tactics and must never rely on one course of action throughout a game. Tunnel vision quickly leads to defeat.

THE BAD
The rules are a bit fiddly and not written intuitively - it is practically impossible to find a certain rule in the book in a timely manner. Facts and figures are hidden in large blocks of rambling words, so that finding the answer to any particular question is like looking for a 3000-year lost ring in Middle-earth.

THE UGLY
I'm going blind with the small print.Argh.

RATING: 10

[1st Ed] I have played this beauty of a game over 70 times now and I still get immense satisfaction from playing competitive opponents. There are lots of things to love about this game but a few things have increasingly annoyed me:
1. I dislike the stupid, childish miniatures! Just give me nicely drawn cardboard chits.
2. It gets harder to read those cards.
3. We need an official master rule book with all the FAQs already answered. Its gets tiring looking in various places to get answers, and every game I have to look something up. I've decided to lower my rating because the rules are difficult to understand.
4. I am definitely not a fan of FFG style games.

Without Event Cards, the game would really just be Risk with a few recognizable characters thrown in and a time limit."  Which is what I felt like I was playing except those "event" cards eluded to above seem to be wildly unbalanced.  This is NOT worth the hype. If you are a "dyed-in-the-wool" Rings fan, you'll probably love it to death.  I still can't figure out why have all those lovely different miniatures when all the armies are generic.  The map is pretty, but almost useless as a gaming tool.  Again, we get lots of cashing in on the latest movie theme, but at least it was an effort, unlike lots of the garbage that gets produced after a big motion picture.  I could be convinced to try it again, but I'm not eagerly awaiting the coming of the Fellowship.

RATING: N/A

We finallly set it up and slugged through a few turns (that much took over 2 hours). Husband as the Free Peoples, me as the Shadow. I'll take the big rulebook to bed for a few nights, and maybe I'll be ready for a full game soon.

We did find that the minatures of the same colour are very difficult to tell apart at a glance (i.e.: the Northerners vs. Rohan vs. Gondor vs. Elves), but maybe that comes with time. Or time plus a considerable amount of model paint. Oh, and the Nazgul are badly balanced, they keep toppling over onto their soldiers. That's only funny the first three times.

RATING: 9.5

All the elements combine to lead the players to follow the story arc of the books, yet there is always the delicious tension of having to manage your dice/cards/units to accomplish what you know you have to do.

Two games in one: an operational level war game alongside a character game that both sides must focus on to be successful. Within the story arc you can choose various strategies and tactics ensuring ample replayablity. Only a few niggling minor gripes prevent this from being a perfect "ten."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
UPDATE: Played this again recently, and I'm to break all my rules and give it a 6.5. Usually, on my rating scale, a '7' is a game that is probably a keeper if it offers something interesting and different, while a '6' rating essentially guarantees the game will be sold. War of the Ring seems like the absolutely perfect bubble game. I want it and its huge box out of my house to make room for better games, and yet when it comes to selling it, I can never quite pull the trigger, and when I play it, I have just barely enough fun to still not be able to make up my mind on what to do. So I keep it. But I'm not thrilled about it. So much stress for such an average game. If it weren't Tolkien, it would be gone.
---
Overall, disappointing. War of the Ring was on an odd power curve for me. First game was disappointing, as the use of theme here is not very imaginative - this is Risk with a War of the Ring veneer. It's a pretty good veneer, it should be said, but it really hasn't grasped the theme fundamentally the way Knizia's Lord of the Rings did. But the next couple plays were fun, the dice add some excitement, the game is visually appealing, and it's familiar enough via its Risk heritage to be accessible.

Where the game got killed for me, though, was in the repeat draw. War of the Ring as only one really valid path for the Fellowship player: race the Fellowship as fast as possible. This then badly constrains the Shadow Player's options - you can't seriously hunt for the ring, it's all about getting the military victory conditions as fast as possible. This then degenerates into a game that isn't about judging your opponents, adapting, or other elements of real strategy, strategy elements that a game this long really has to have - the strategies are pretty much set - but about hyper-technical tactical details and analysis. For me, this is OK, but not something I can really get into, and certainly is not very thematic.

At the end of the day, the game is just too expensive for the limited replay value for me. Unless you're going to get really excited by the minute tactical details of this one, probably something to play on a friend's copy.

I think War of the Ring fell over in two critical ways. Firstly, as mentioned, the theme simply isn't grasped firmly eough. Secondly, though, is the action dice. They are a fairly clever as a concept, but when viewed in a holistic way, I think they are ineffective. They serve to constrain rather than enable. You have an idea what you want to do, but you are constrained by what the dice allow you to do. Certain combinations of dice don't suddenly open up options that weren't available before, although many combinations will prevent you from doing what you want. If they enabled uncertainty or an ability to bluff, that might be something - but they don't, all dice rolls are open and can be seen by everyone, so your opponent knows exactly when you have a crappy roll and how to hammer you for it. The Fellowship knows how dangerous it is to move, and can calculate the odds exactly, which seems quite wrong.

Obviously, I'm not exactly enamored of the game, but I actually liked the 3-player version better than the 2-player. This version adds more constraints on how you can play (usually not a good thing), but by not allowing the FP player to take consecutive actions with the same nation, it seems to weaken Gondor and Rohan enough to make the game a little more interesting. By contrast, the SP doesn't seem to suffer much from the new restrictions, and they benefit a little bit with the added card-drawing freedom.

Addenda: whoever the graphic design person was on this, I certainly hope he or she never works again in the game industry. The number of serious usability issues is absolutely mind-blowing, from areas so small they are unusable despite vast areas of empty and wasted space, impossible-to-distinguish-or-even-see towns/cities/fortifications, indisinguishable Free People sculpts, invisible terrain and boder symbols, to tiny and meaningless iconography, and miniscule font sizes. The game *looks* good, so I'm not sure how significant this will be to most people, but the actual design is exceptionally poor.

RATING: 10
Wow.  Right now there's no game I'm more consumed with.  This is by far my favorite wargame of all time.  Combines elements of a fantasy adventure sub-plot within a well architected wargame.  Rules can be tough to crack.  I would suggest playing the introductory game a few times, per the suggestion in the rules.  My one gripe is that I really wish the units had been colored differently.  Common sentiment.  Be prepared for a time investment, but it's worth it.

UPDATE:  I have played it nine times now, and it still holds at a 10.  A further gripe, though, is that the rules are tricky to learn.  I would recommend looking through the FAQs, etc, as there are lots of rules that are non-obvious but which affect gameplay out greatly for one side or the other.  Remember that if a character dies (which can never happen if they're alone in a region), you lose the character dice associated with them.  For instance, for Gandalf, Aragorn, Witch King, etc.  If the Fellowship gets revealed, they can't end movement in a settlement.  Also, the game isn't over until the end of the turn for military victories, so it's possible to lose ground, then gain it back before you will lose the game.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
[i]For what it's worth as context, I am not a huge Tolkien fan; I think The Hobbit is a pretty good kids' story, I find LOTR entertaining as literary comfort food once I give myself permission to ruthlessly skip ahead when the prose starts turning an especially deep shade of purple or, worse, when somebody starts threatening to break out into [b]another fucking song[/b], and I can't imagine any non-gunpoint context under which I would read the Silmarillion.[/i]

So.

Neither the total catastrophe I feared nor the miracle I had faint forlorn hope of it being. War of the Ring is a decent, playable-in-a-longish evening (we started the setup and rules explanations a little after five and were done a little after nine) dudes-on-a-map game. There's a lot of dice chucking, which some people object to, which is a mystery to me -- surely the game would be more random if there were only a few die rolls that were considerably more decisive?

Likewise, the rolling of a fistful of dice every turn, which some people can't seem to get past, was OK by me; it gave each turn a little shape right from the start, and gave you a partial preview of what your opponent was likely to do, thus informing your in-turn tactics a bit without making the whole experience run on rails. (I found the dice pool of available actions to be less restrictive to play than the orders cards in Combat Commander, with the caveat that CC's order cards are a hell of a lot more thematically justified.)

I [i]do[/i] think that some sort of gamey simultaneous action selection system a la Race For The Galaxy might be more interesting than just dicing for the available actions, but the game does provide you with enough outs that you'll very rarely be in a position where you cannot at all move an army that absolutely must be moved.

Combat is more buckets o' dice; it felt comfortingly familiar to me as a Here I Stand player, really, and it works fine.

I enjoyed the hunt for the Ring/moving the Fellowship subgame; it gives both sides another dimension to worry about besides what's happening on the board plus it seems like it's pretty much the only plausible winning scenario for the Free People in most games. Mechanically, I don't have any great objections to any aspect of the game, in the final analysis, and I would prefer this to the old Milton Bradley Gamesmaster games, which are more or less this game's peers, complexity- and mechanics-wise.

My lack of enthusiasm for the game stems less from something wrong with the way it's put together than from a lack of interest, both in this style of game in general (strategy-light dudes on a map), and with the source material, plus, strange as it is to say this about a game that drizzles fancy components in the box like Rachel Ray drizzles olive oil on a roasting chicken, a pretty searing general dislike/annoyance with the components.

The board is as large a mounted map as you'll ever encounter and yet far too small for all the bits likely to be put into key spaces like Minas Tirith, the spaces on the board do not obviously display at a glance whether they contain a town vs. a city vs. a fortification (and none of the information about a space is legible if there are troops there (and there are ALWAYS troops on pretty much any space that matters) the cards can be difficult to read, the dice (while admittedly lovely bits of pseudo-marble shiny) have unintuitive squiggly symbols on them that are hard to read across a table, the hero figures for each side are indistinguishable from one another without consulting a key, the political tokens are likewise virtually indistinguishable without a long look and a cross-check against a interpretive guide, and, worst of all, the Dudes on this particular map are all red and blue and distinguished only by shape, this despite the fact that the particular faction within a given side that a unit belongs to is frequently critical information, information that that it is thus virtually impossible to get without detailed examination of individual pieces. Also my Nazgul kept toppling over dammit.

In short, everything about the graphic design of all the physical bits values a very narrow band of aesthetics over legibility and usability. I wouldn't harp on this for the better parts of two paragraphs if this wasn't a game specifically designed to look tantalizingly cool on the tabletop and if it wasn't priced accordingly. And all of this sexy cool designer porn is exactly orthagonal to the basic goal of usability.

And I throw out, perhaps churlishly, the data point that we players of historical wargames with our quotidian, color-coded, cardboard counters and our point-to-point maps have figured this stuff out a long time ago. Your funeral, 'trashers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a war game that feels a lot like Risk or Axis and Allies except there are multiple victory conditions and a distinct unbalance in forces that really raises the tension in the game. The use of the LoTR theme is fantastic. One player takes the Shadow Armies and the other player takes the Free Peoples. It is the free peoples job to survive long enough to get the ring bearers to mount doom (or possibly to take two of Sauron's strongholds... more on that later). The game starts with a ton of pieces on the board. The miniatures are fairly well detailed and fun to play with, but I was a bit disappointed in how bent up they came out of the box. Also, they don't fit on the board especially well.

During play, players roll dice to see what actions are available to them in any given round. Depending on what actions they take, there is also dice rolling involved for attacking, defending, and hunting for the ring bearers. The dice rolls usually require a 1/3 or 1/6 chance of success, but can get more favorable if the free peoples try to move the ring too fast.

It is the dice rolling that is the ultimate failure for this game for me. Sure, they add an element of luck and fun for a gamer who wants a less hardcore experience, but they also mean that the players turn a great deal of their destiny over to random chance. Depending on how the dice roll up, either side can score an easy victory quickly. For my part, I am left with a bitter taste in my mouth and the feeling that whatever I did the dice squashed.

If you like Risk and don't mind what the dice will do to you, this is a great game. If you like LoTR at all, this is a great game. If you want a deep strategic experience where you control your own destiny, this is not a great game. Try Agricola or Puerto Rico or something like that. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I have questions about the replayability after some time.  But at first blush the game seems exceptionally balanced.  The components are very very nice and add wonderfully to the theme.  But this game is not all shiny bits....there is some substance under all that.  In the couple games I've played, War of the Rings builds a slow and steady crescendo of tension and drama.  The games can go either way and reward clever play.  There is a bit of luck, but I don't find it to be a large enough ingredient to distract from the richness of the game.  

We played one game where each side teetered on the edge of oblivion for about 3 turns and the Fellowship managed to destroy the ring at the last possible moment...and then just by the skin of their teeth. It literally couldn't have been closer. We got done, looked at each other and said, "Wow!"  That's what a game should be.

It is tense exciting and different every time, and the feeling of play is very authentically "Tolkien". Components, cards and map are excellent and look beautiful on setup. Some of my figures were very bent (I bought 2 copies and both were the same), but this is easily remedied with the application of hot water and careful patient bending. (but see below) The figures themselves are remarkably detailed and well designed (though the large Nazgul are top heavy and WILL continually fall over in play - we found this entertaining, but it will frustrate others) In summary, I cannot imagine any game being produced to a better standard, and while no game is ever "perfect", this one is as close as you're ever likely to get. Buy it!  Update - reduced my rating to "9" because I found over time the figures "mended" with the hot water method above will sometines re-assert their original bent shape. Seems to be because of the "rubbery" type plastic used. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Good game, but complicated and very long.

RATING: 8
I'm finally playing it.After a horrendous wait of 14 months (!!) Why? one reason! The Manual. (Dutch Phalanx version, but I doubt it to be due to the translation) The rules of this game are written in the most awful & chtulian mind bending format imaginable! Too much for words! The format they adapt is a disaster for any organised mind! Everything is mixed up. They over elaborate so you totally lose track. And when you go looking for a part of the rules and (by luck) localise the title you re looking for, you have a 50% chance to find your answer. If not, you have to go look for the next title (identical but different lettering) for your answer and even then.. I say different lettering, but there is actually correspondence between chapters or parts and lettering. I hope this rulebook is easy for non gamers, but for me (the gamer person who usually reads the manual & then proceeds in teaching anybody else I play with) it was HELL! I'm used to reading a manual (sometimes twice) and then dive in! Needless to say I read this bitch trough once, ended up totally weirded out, cancel the first game (4 days after getting it) & then finally playing it Now; 14 months and 28 days after getting it!This 30 page rulebook have been 15 pages at most if for example the FF people would have had a go at it! *edit: And they actually DID make one :) Altough the size is about the same, the clarity is day and night! If only I had looked there earlier!* The only way to learn this is to find a friend & prepare to dig trough the rulebook as you play it. Taking about 2 hours for the first and second turn! Do yourself a favour and get some rule summaries / charts / .. player-aids! They ll do you A LOT of good!The 30 minutes of "preparing the board" are gruesome. This ll probably go quicker as we get a hang of it, but still.. its a whole lot of Rosie .. ehrm plastic to spread out over that Huge board!Enough gibberish, the game itself is Great! Rounds take about 15 minutes to complete and its kinda thrilling. I have the feeling the good guys don't really stand a chance, but then again.. that's authentic :) A well deserved 8 although I fear the average 6 hours (according to friends) will keep this one on the shelf more that it deserves!

RATING: 8
I greatly enjoy this game on two levels:

1. The game is a fun one. The initial position eliminates much of the "build up forces" time that makes some games take forever; the action-die mechanics are neat; there are interesting tactical and strategic decisions to make; there are many ways to try things.

2. It *feels* right, with the free peoples stalling for time, the fellowship fighting for every inch of ground, the overwhelming hordes of darkness inexorably tightening their grip, and it all riding (eventually) on a pair of hobbits. The mechanics support the atmosphere.

I have a few minor irritations (drawing useless action cards early or late in the game, etc.) but my only major complaint is that separating off a bunch of Companions to get involved with the military side of things - something that's both interesting for gameplay and reflects what happened in the books - tends to be a terrible strategy, as it both slows down the Fellowship (by using Character dice) and makes the ringbearer more vulnerable (by removing companions who could defend them).

I've only played with the expansion game once; it looks like it adds even more good stuff.

RATING: 9
I hate hype, which is partially why it took me 2 years to try this game. In fact, it's an excellent, extremely tight, thematic game with well integrated mechanics. WOTR works on a number of levels, adding a level of complexity uncommon to most Ameritrash (which, ultimately, is what it is). The Fellowship "mini-game within a game" is an extraordinary mechansim for adding a sense of tension to every decision, and also a convenient game timer (much, much better than the arbitrary end point after X-many turns in so many lesser games). Downsides are primarily that the game plays looong, especially when you're still learning (expect 6-8 hours, even after a couple of games), and a fairly dense rulebook (although not unreasonably so).

RATING: N/A
Comments after 1st game: All I can say so far is I don't like the organization of the rules (no concise place that says what each action die's choices are, you have to dig through the rest of the rules to see that, "oh, I can do this with an X die".  Also, I hate that several of the nation's army types look so similar.  The bases need to be a different color if the shapes are so similar.  The map is pretty but barely functional, the national borders are hard to distinguish and finding regions is a pain, esp. those that aren't in the initial setup and thus numbered in the rules.  It took an hour just to set up and still we kept grabbing the wrong armies.  All that said, I think there is a fun game in here, maybe after we paint all the bases, write in the initial starting armies ON THE BOARD and download a bunch of rule rewrites we will be able to find it more easily.
Comments after 2nd game:  I still don't know how to rate this.  Setup was faster now that we had baggied everything.  The game started out well, we split it over two nights.  But, by night 2 and the hobbits approaching Mordor we began to think not all was well in Middle Earth.  What is the purpose of all those troops in the Mordor regions, the game isn't long enough for them to get anywhere and they can't actually hurt the fellowship because it fast-tracks to the Mountain as soon as it reaches the edge region.  The fellowship player really has to decide to either bee-line for Mt. Doom or go the military victory route, they won't have time to do both.  In our game, the Fellowship never moved more than a couple armies (and then it was in response to a Shadow whompin' down in Gondor / Rohan territory).  Once the fellowship made it to Mt. Doom I had to stop my military conquest to try to corrupt it to death, but that was all pretty random and didn't work.  He only drew one tile with a "stop" on it so got to the top pretty quickly.
As others have said, this is a better LOTR simulation than a game.  There are lots of fun aspects to the game but they don't fit together well at all.  I enjoy the card play and military aspects but the fellowship part just sucked the fun out.  I think I ought to try Queens Gambit based on the other negative comments, maybe it shares some of the good things and not so much of the bad.  And, I still think the rules are horribly organized and riddled with conflicts.

RATING: 8.5
A blast to play, this combines a light wargame with a Euro-like quest game in a package of awesome Tolkien flavor.  It'll take a few more plays to really determine how many of the strategies are obvious as opposed to being real choices, but the evidence points to there being enough of the latter to make this one a winner for the long term.  However, it won't appeal to Eurogamers who dislike light wargames unless they are LotR SuperFans.

The color scheme used for the pieces was a very, very bad idea.  It can be difficult to tell many of them apart.  Fine for those with the time and inclination to paint 'em all, but a hassle for the rest of us.

UPDATE: Wow, 5 years since my original review! Considering the amount of effort the game takes to play, the strategies are not quite as deep as they might be (it's mostly "how do I best make do with what I've rolled"), but it's so much fun to play that I've upped my rating a bit. This might be the most flavorful board game of all time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Ueber super game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This game looks good and has some nice ideas such as the dice for actions mechanic, and is probably a good teaching game for people new to wargaming or younger players. However, when you get right down to it, the game boils down to how well the tiles fall once the fellowship reaches Mordor. All the time, effort and cards you spent maneuvering (what little maneuvering there is at 1 area per turn), mustering, battling and sieging is often, ultimately, a complete waste. This game is a case study in how a great theme (not to mention plastic bits) can obscure mediocre mechanics and pacing.

Lastly, as others have pointed out, this game will not stand up to repeated plays. There is only 1 path to victory for each player, and the rest is up to the action dice and cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Luck (dice rolls, card draws) are too important in determining the winner, and took a bit away from our enjoyment. It was also a bit too long to keep us interested.

RATING: 3.75
Why did I ever let anyone talk me into trying this?  I only played this game once but it had WAY WAY too many rules.  There was not enough clarity in the rules.  There were tons of extraneous rules to remember that had little to no effect on the over-all outcome of the 4+ hour game.  I am lost as to how so many geeks give this game a 10!  I have to assume they would like almost anything with the Lord of the Ring theme and tons of cool components (which I do like for the most part).  The geek rating is certainly NOT based upon the elegence of the game mechanics.  I just assume that people really enjoyed the experience of playing the game because it brought the Tolkien world to life for them.  Maybe if I had more free time the experience would have been better for me too.  Instead, it was tedious and laborious.  I would love to see this game remade as a 1 - 2 hour game (like Puerto Rico) and with the elegance of what I have grown to expect from the best European style games.

RATING: 9
Almost the game I wanted on this subject and at this scale, but not quite. I agree with those who complain about the size of the card text and that some of the town/city icons could be clearer. I'm also not a big fan of the board generally - Middle-earth, at least most of the west of it, should be greens rather than the drab tans and browns. I don't like the figures much either. They are good sculpts, although the Nazghul are a little impractical for a crowded game board, but the colours are all over the place. They are too bright and mixed up - I would have preferred all of the units from the same race to be of the same colour and to have been of a less jarring hue - the orcs should have been black, the Urukai brown etc. In fact I think I would have preferred the whole thing with cardboard counters with good quality art on them. Just aesthetics maybe but it does result in some playability issues and in a game where theme is so important so are the aesthetics in conjouring up that theme.

These whinges aside it is still a good game and is easily the best Tolkein game that allows you to move armies/characters at a strategic level across a map of middle-earth. Despite my gripes it still captures the theme well and the combat mechanic is much superior to that of Risk, which was a worry I had on first reading the rules. The theme adds at least a mark for me and if you like boardgames and Tolkein this really is a must buy. 

It's a bit like the movie - not without it's faults but still excellent and the best that we can really expect on an immensely difficult to realise, but cherished, subject.

RATING: 10
This game is perfection.  It beautifully captures the Lord of the Rings story.  It uses an simple and elegant dice action point allowance system that works fluidly and allows for a different game every time.  The theme is pervasive and at no point do the mechanics pull you out of the story that is developing.  There is constant tension as the Free Peoples attempt to sneak past the massive armies of Sauron.  This is a permanent fixture in my collection.

RATING: 5
Huh.  How is this possibly in the top ten?  Wife and I thought it did capture the feeling of LotR; I was totally stressed as Sauron needing just one more city before Gimli and the ring made it to the end.  But for the long amount of time the game takes, it just didn't feel fun.  I think the dice-action mechanic takes too much choice away from you.  So the game is driving itself towards the conclusion and we are just watching.  How about something like an AP system where it gets progressively more expensive to pick the same action. Like 1-2-4 AP, and Good gets 4AP and Evil gets 7AP, just like the number of dice.  That way, you'd be choosing what happens and actually playing the game.

RATING: 10
This game deserves the hype.  There are so many tough choices.  So many things you want to do with limited options.  And the theme is amazing.   However one drawback I found initially was the fact that the spaces are too small for all the pieces.  Even using chips (ala Axis and Allies) to substitute for the figures doesn't free up enough space and makes things clunky.  This can have a negative impact on strategy as the pieces unknowingly bleed over to adjacent spaces.  And the Nazgul look cool, but they are too tall and top-heavy...they are constantly falling over. I fixed it by taping pennies to the bottom of them.  Also I wish the print on the event cards was larger.However, this one is still going strong after 6 plays with good replayability. I always want to play this incredible game...and I don't expect it to change anytime soon.  This one is a 10 now (and has moved from 7->8->9->10).  The small spaces are not a big deal once you get used to them.Update...after 13 plays this is now my favorite game.  I didn't think any game would supplant Dune, but this matches Dune in every category except diplomacy.  Still, I find myself thinking about playing WotR more than Dune nowdays.  Everyone I've taught the game to has also liked it.

Update:  The new release has gotten even better and fixed the few minor complaints that I had.  I mean they fixed everything that I suggested.  Larger cards. Extra Dwarven elite deployed during startup.  Troop box and army markers (to reduce overcrowding).  Better map layout that shows adjacencies more clearly.  Shorter Nazgul.  Fixed wording on cards.  If I have to find a negative it would be worse quality dice and maybe the mini's are a little too bendable.  But cmon...the game itself is a true 10...and now the components are 99% perfect.  A true 'must have' in any serious gamer's collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Lovely looking game themed on the War of the Ring.  Players represent the Fellowship against the Shadow Alliance.  This breaks down into Elves, North, Dwarves, Gondor and Rohan up against Isengard, Mordor and Haradrim/Easterlings.  Dice are tossed that generate actions, akin to being dealt a hand of cards in a card driven wargame.  Actions are spent on recruiting, campaigning, playing events or on the ring game.  The ring game is an abstracted journey from Rivendell to Sauron's Smithy, with Sauron dogging the footsteps and tossing corruption at the Fellowship.  Sauron is trying to recover the ring or win a military victory before the ring is destroyed.  Some issues with the graphics on the board - the town/city/fortress icons are too small, too alike, and disappear as soon as you plonk a few units down - I'm painting up some wooden houses so at least we can see them.  The event cards donate 50% of their area to ornate bordering, with the important text crammed into tiny 5 point font in the remaining 50% - a shame. Still, the gameplay is superb.  The designers obviously understand the book, as all the pressures of force and time are here, and it's been superbly developed into a balanced, thematic game.  This game is a must for Tolkien fans, and worth a try for anyone else.  Great effort.

Second edition (from ARES) is here and fixes several problems - much better graphics and cards you can actually read.  My favourite game, by quite a margin.

RATING: 3
The game has good details that do not make up for the fundamental flaws that ruin it: 1) the Fellowship player has far less activity than the Mordor player, 2) a dice roll at the start of each turn determines your strategic direction and you only make tactical responses to these dice, hence it felt like the game was playing me instead of vice versa 3) the game resembled "Star Wars: Queens' Gambit" moreso than any other game, lacking new mechanisms or interactions. A derivative of Risk with action cards that attempt to add flavor of the books to a bland wargame. Like many recent "light wargames", it is focused on memorizing and playing the overpowered cards instead of strategic thinking.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I'd call WOTR a cross between Risk, the LotR Boardgame, and Star Wars: Queen's Gambit.  Overall, it most closely resembles the last of those.  QG is also an asymmetrical wargame where one side applies overwhelming force while the other attempts to stall long enough to complete their side quest.  QG also limits your available actions each turn, but uses cards rather than dice.  My wife and I really enjoy QG, but WotR is a richer, more rewarding experience.  

The game is all about playing well to maximize your odds of winning the race through Mordor.  The fact that you can play very well yet still lose due to bad Mordor tile draws is a plus for me, not a detriment.  After all, if the endgame is determined before the end, why bother playing it through at all?  However, skillful play definitely dramatically increases your odds of ultimately winning.  Sometimes as the FP especially I feel like the Hunt in Mordor was tragically unfair, but upon retrospection I realize that I should have spent longer healing before plunging into Mordor.  The luxury to do that, is of course provided by doing well on the military front.  I really love the interaction of the military and the hunt for the ring components of the game.

I still find this game to be a new challenge each time, as the players must shape their strategies around the cards they have available, and I've not yet come close to exhausting the interesting combinations that can arise.

Plus, my wife loves the game.

I think I would be willing to pay for a fully painted set of miniatures on this one!  

A few drawbacks - the die rolling here is generally exciting and fair overall.  However, as with any game with dice, a string of outlier rolls can tip the game.  Also, I did not find this to be playable out of the box - I had to take quite a bit of time to color the bases of the figures.  The map is also a bit hard to manage sometimes, and the cardboard counter to replace the figures don't work really well.  This might be inevitable, however, as I suppose the map couldn't be much bigger, and I wouldn't want smaller miniatures (or would I?  Actually smaller miniatures that came in distinct colors for each nation would have been fine with me!)

Overall, my wife and I always look forward to playing this and will center weekend afternoons around it.  We have managed to play games of this in under 2 hours, but in general you should allow 3-4.

UPDATE May 2011: I toyed with giving this game a 10 for a while, but in the end can't do it for two reasons. 1) It is too fiddly - I still need to hit the rulebook and FAQ every game.  2) It is either has too much of the game outcome dependent on luck, or it needs to be shorter.  
Don't get me wrong, thing is an great epic game, but isn't perfect.  Also, -0.5 points because you pretty much have to paint the figures some how to tell the armies apart.  Couldn't they have molded in different color plastics?

UPDATE UPDATE (5 whole days later): Screw it, WotR is a 10.  This is one of those rare games that gives me a visceral excitement n my gut just at the thought of playing it.  The game may not be perfect, but love ain't always easy.  Still minus half a point for making me paint all the minis just to play, though (Collector's Edition gets the full 10!)

RATING: 9
If there is a better narrative-driven wargame, I have not seen it.   A most impressive design, with all the story elements in there, and yet you will find yourself always mixing things up.  Like, "Remember when Boromir made it back to Gondor alive?" or "when Legolas stayed with Frodo and Sam into Mordor?".   All of that kind of stuff is possible in this game.

With two ways to win for each player, and dual-use cards, there is a lot of strategic tug-of-war happening in this game.  The momentum builds solidly every turn and the last track in Mordor is a constant thrill-ride while you await every tile draw with heavy anticipation.  

The rulebook is a bit of a wreck, but once you see how the game works, it's not all that hard.   Fantastic fun, and the most thematically-rich wargame I own.  Each time I play it, it gets better.

RATING: 8
Very interesting way to balance the two sides. One side has all the numerical advantages, but he has more grounds to cover. 

Consider a shopping mall and a thief. The shopping mall has staggeringly more resources to spend on security, but they must be on vigilance 24-7, and cover the entire mall. The thief has the luxury of choosing where and when to make his move, and succeed despite being overwhelmingly outnumbered by man-work hours.

RATING: 7
A great game in every way except for one important area: we found it to have very little replay value.

Wading through that awkwardly constructed rulebook was a chore, but worth the effort.  And there are some great play-aids available for this game here on the 'Geek.

But after playing it a few times, we felt that it was always the same.  The Fellowship races like hell to dump the ring before the bad guys can win the military war.  We found that trying to win the war as the Free Peoples was just too hard to pull off, especially against a Sauron player with any sort of experience who knows what to watch for.  And as the Sauron player, trying to find the ring was a waste of resources when it was always so much easier to concentrate on winning the war instead.

Don't get me wrong, we didn't always get the same outcome in game after game; it's just that we found ourselves always using the same strategies for each side.  Although this game offers you "multiple paths to victory," the odds of victory were always far greater if you followed Path A rather than Path B, and that makes Path B seem like a red herring they crammed into the game just so it wouldn't get boring.

Great game in terms of theme, but the movies didn't help it any in that regard.  It's really hard to play the board game and NOT have it mirror the plot of the movie...

RATING: 6
A few plays.  Lots of theme; great sculpts.  A number of rules exceptions to learn.  The game is railroaded too close to the book for my tastes—-the Fellowship player’s only real option for victory seems to be to march Frodo to Mt Doom using one of two viable paths (Moria or high pass). I would have preferred more possibilities for military strategic options for the FP, and/or more “what if” alternate scenarios and victory conditions for more variety (e.g., FP able to use the Ring, or other ways to destroy the Ring), even if that would mean deviations from the book.

For the FP, the game seems more like a Fellowship character game than a wargame; military strategy is purely defensive/reactionary, and is mainly just recruiting in response to SP activity; and there is simply too little for the FP to do compared to the SP, so that it can get boring.  The FP and SP players almost seem to be playing separate games at times, with only indirect interaction in each others’ spheres—-the FP concentrating on Fellowship character stuff, the SP concentrating on military action.  The military action is a bit too abstracted--I wanted more of a military game.  Progressing through Mordor seems too abstract and random with tile draws (and difficult).

I like the action dice mechanism: it creates interesting tactical decisons each turn, as do the cards, although I wonder at the utility of many of the FP event cards.  My feeling from playing the FP is that the game may be too repetitive in an overall sense; the game always seems to cover the same basic pattern (SP conquers several strongholds, Frodo gets to Mordor). Although there are some nice tactical variation and nuances from game to game.

RATING: 7
Yes, this is a fantastic game to look at. Yes, this game has multiple paths to victory. Yes, this game does have a feel for LotR rather than a tacked on theme. But after a couple of plays I find it hard to understand the high ratings. It's long...very long. Set up is tedious. If you play with a different opponent each time it will add an hour to the game and basic questions will still be asked throughout. It's very hard to differentiate between the Free Peoples troops, the cards can be confusing and often plays are missed because they aren't intuitive. Overall, it rates high on production and quality and quite a bit less in the critical area of playability. I'll try it several more times and perhaps my rating will go up in the future.

RATING: 6
Great!  A great, epic game -- the quintessential LOTR wargaming experience, and maybe a quintessential AT experience.  Asymmetrical setup and powers show a clear understanding of the theme and a respect for the source material.  Held back from greatness by a few cludgy rules and a mediocre rulebook, which requires that you either play it all the time, or spend 20-30 minutes on a refresher course with your opponent.  The pieces could also be made a little easier to tell apart.    

But these are nitpicks!  LOTR is the ultimate fantasy yarn, and WOTR is the ultimate LOTR game (although Knizia's LOTR is equally awesome in totally different ways).

RATING: 10
I suspect I've overrated this game, but it's hard to fault a game that gets the epic feel of LotR right.  It doesn't do everything right, but I can't imagine how one could make an epic strategy game of the war of the ring that does things better than this.  The feeling of being the Free People's player, watching the armies of Sauron bear down on you, all the while struggling to move the ring forward, trying to hide what you're doing and buying the time to get the ring to Mount Doom is fantastic.  The event cards generally capture key events from the book well, allowing exciting reversals for both sides.  It does seem a little unbalanced, but then things weren't really balanced in the books, so this hardly bothers me.

RATING: N/A
Best Tolkien themed war-game.  2-4 players, 3 hours, high complexity 5/5.  Is it a Risk/Allis-Allies style game, as some suggest?  According to my well-informed and reliable buddy, this is an unfair characterization of the game, because it is totally unique and brilliantly contrived to fit the books very well.
Impressions after two plays: the assymetrical aspect of the game is well done - each player has different objectives, yet the game seems well balanced. The various plot lines (military conquest by Sauron's forces, ring dunking by Fellowship, potential for corruption) seem well worked together and very thematic, giving a real sense of the drama of the original story, and potential for a tense finish. See also my sesson report of game #2: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/329042
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
WotR is truly a masterpiece of a game, which befits the grandeur of Tolkien's imaginative world perfectly! Strategic storytelling and military decisions with some luck-driven (aka dice roll and card draw) factors to keep every new game fresh, exciting and different leads to good replayability, and ever-changing stories which unfold during the game. Some may be true to the original plot of the books, some will differ quite a lot, allowing a sort of "What if...?" alternate history. The best thing, though, is that while playing you will eventually find yourself immersed in Middle Earth and its struggles of good vs. evil. A true gem!

RATING: 8
[u]The Good[/u]: 1) Has clever mechanics that represent the flight of the ring bearer. 2) Does a good job at touching on many of the major events that take place in the books, and yet still allows the players to forge their own path.

[u]The Bad[/u]: Has less depth than I would have prefered. If it were a bit less Risk-like in the way it handled its units, even without a true solitaire mode this game would easily be in my top 3.

[u]The Bottomline[/u]: Highly recommended for Lord of the Rings fans, and really shines when both players are well familiar with the setting.

RATING: 7
This game is really long, and non-hardcore gamers will likely balk at the ruleset.  That said, seasoned wargamers will find plenty of familiar concepts here.  Basically the good guys have to dunk the ring before the bad guys' inevitable military victory, requiring a balanced approach on the good guys' part (resisting just enough to delay defeat, but also keeping the focus on sneaking to Mt. Doom).  Given the game length and overall complexity, it's a little surprising they didn't go even further in the complexity direction.  I have only played this as a 2-player game.

The 3-4 player rules are somewhat laughable, as they simply divide a side's army in half and give each to a different player.  I can see how that would affect the pacing of board movement, and admit there might be more of a difference than I can predict, but if I have more than 1 opponent available for that much time, I'll probably look for a game where everyone can serve a more selfish interest.

RATING: 7
I feel one will either love or hate this game, as its one of the worst dice-fests to ever grace my table.  However it also has some of the best implemented themes I've ever seen, and really forces the players to become involved in the alternative Middle-Earth history they are creating.  this is one of those games you need a whole day to complete, and I've yet to see a game last under four hours, especially since the rules are incredibly complex, and the rulebook tends to be frustratingly vague, confusingly worded and badly organized.  There is also a lot of downtime as well as potential for frustration due to the over-reliance on dice and random card draws.  This aside, its one of those few games that creates an organic story out of a gameplay session that you will probably be taking about with your friends for a while after.  Not something to play frequently, but a must-play for fans of Tolkien, or epic gaming adventures.

RATING: 8
This game uses great elements from many existing systems in a nicely streamlined way.  The turn sequence and choices during a turn are interesting enough to sustain a certain tension throughout the game and I like this very much.  While the dice provide random choices which keeps the game from being too predictable, it is my impression that there are probably few good openings but many different ways of executing them.  But I think enough experimenting would underscore a few obligatory moves on both players.  Still, I find the drama of every turn to be enticing.  However...

Who has the audacity to take wonderful John Howe art and ruin it with what is definitely the worst design of a map I have ever seen.  I am convinced this was not picked in playtesting simply because the testing was done with counters or such.  Incidentally, GIVE ME COUNTERS AND NOT TOYS!!!  Those figurines are klunky and impede the game; good old counters would have been perfectly suited for this game.  Would have made the game cheaper and not one bit less fun.

RATING: 8
Update:  Played some more and raised it from a 7 to an 8.  I could really see diving into this game with the right player (hopefully my son in a year or so).  The artwork and theme are inviting and it's fun just to push armies across that big board.  I think the action dice thing is a nice change of pace from other games.  I can't claim to rate this game on it's strategies or depth because I haven't been there yet, but it does capture the fun of a risk style area movement game that I loved as a kid.  Of course the theme and artwork is a huge draw for me!


Update:  I cubed it.  I kept the plastic elite troops and leaders and used wooden cubes for the lower ranks.  I also spray painted units to make it easier to tell things apart.  It really cleaned up the board and made it easier to read the map.  It's much more inviting to play now. 

Only one play, could go up.  I'm not wild about the crowded plastic figures.  Still, fun so far...  I really like the big map with all it's areas.  I might convert the plastic figures to wooden cubes just to uncrowd the game.  I'm a sucker for big, beautiful area maps.  I recomend it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
[Light Wargame, Risk Clone - Dice for Combat, Dice for Actions, Event Cards, Plastic Minis] [2P:3] Inelegant. Clunky. Unintuitive. Frustrating graphic design decisions. Worst of all for a dyed-in-the-wool JRRT fan, War of the Ring only superficially captures the feel of Middle Earth for all that chrome. Could definitely have done without a lot of the fancy bits (What the heck is the point of having all the fancy figs if you already know they won't fit on the map and so you need to have the cardboard chits - just use the chits and scratch the figs! Oh that's right we need to figs to up sales to unsuspecting people will ooh and ahh at them. The mini/chit juggling lengthened the game and made it so fiddly that the next time we played we left the minis in the box. What a crying waste. And what's the point of having all the different looking figures of THEY'RE ALL THE SAME? ARGH!) and pretty symbols in exchange for streamlining and usability. Did they REALLY have to have different symbols for the good and evil dice? Perhaps I've been spoiled by the simple elegance of The Confrontation, which has much more JRRT flavor in fifteen minutes than WOTR does in three hours. WotR is clearly outclassed by H:RvC and HotS from a playability standpoint; disappointing to say the least. The game is extremely RANDOM RANDOM RANDOM and has a disproportionate amount of compounded LUCK LUCK LUCK, even for a wargame. Dice to determine your actions (unforgivable), Event card draws (with some questionable balance in the cards), dice to resolve combat, dice to resolve the search for the ring, and to finish it off, a tile draw to determine the effects of a successful search. The entire "Hunt" segment of the game is laughable. I'll stop short of calling WotR terrible or broken (it's not), but it's tremendously disappointing, random and luck-driven. As a Tolkein fan, and as a boardgame fan, I cannot recommend this game at all unless you are hopelessly enamored of Risk variants. And by the way - the 3 & 4 player claim is a joke, this is a 2P game only. 3 & 4P simply splits the duties normally handled by a single player among two players. Unless you're comfortable with a very high degree of luck in a 3-hour game, much more so than a quality wargame that plays in the same amount of time (my point of comparison is always AH's Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage, and Columbia's Hammer of the Scots), and you're very tolerant of usability issues in a game's design, I would strongly advise that you at the very least try before you buy. Even if you are a huge Lord of the Rings fan like I am. ***UPDATED: Added weight rating of "light" due to randomness. It doesn't really matter what you do; whoever rolls better and draws better, wins.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game is a mixed bag. It has a number of interesting sub-systems, for insistence its "hiding in plain sight" fellowship procedure, but is seriously marred by its lack of development/design. To name just a few of its most glaring problems:

1. Many cards are far too complex. (Just imagine what it's like having a handful of cards saying something along the lines of, "You can do "A" if you have "B" in area "C," as long as "D" is not in area "E, except when your opponent has already played card "F." And this with a map that makes finding specific areas almost impossible.) In addition, the card's font size is so small you have to wonder if it's even possible to request it that way. (Bring a microscope.)   
2. The figures are indistinguishable. Most of them seem to be either the same color or the same shape. (Bring a powerful microscope). 
3. The markers are all but meaningless. Now I suppose this design decision had something to do with international sales, but why they settled on a political marker system that generates confusion in ALL languages is beyond me. For example, couldn't they have kept the almost squiggles, if they thought so highly of them, and superimposed a capital letter, say "D" for dwarf, "R" for Rohan? (Bring someone with a great memory). 
4. The 2-player game is seriously unbalanced as the Nazgul can be used to reinforce Saruman in an "unhistorical" manner. (Bring house rules).  
5. There is an awful lot going on at once. Elegant it's not. (Only play with someone who knows the rather long rules inside and out). 
6. The game has only limited re-playability as there is really only one strategy for each side to use. So each game revolves around how lucky you are in doing what you tried to do in your last game. And as the armies only move one space at a time (including all-cavalry armies), keeping  your intentions hidden from your opponent is impossible.     

Some quick fixes include keeping 2 or 3 magnifying glasses with your set, using miniature Poker chips a la "Axis and Allies," gluing nickels under the Nazgul to keep them from falling over during play, substituting the "Risk-Lord of the Rings" pieces for those provided (they are color-coded and smaller, so more undersized areas become usable on the rather messy board) and suggesting your group try a different game.   

All in all, a potentially fine game, which could have been 200 percent better with competent development.

RATING: 8
War of The Ring is just the right kind of wargame.  It is dripping with Tolkien theme.  The combat system is dice and battle-card heavy, but interesting and wholly appropriate.  The strategies for victory are many.  While you can't avoid some AP in this game (there are so many choices!) and the unpainted colour scheme of the pieces makes things a little confusing, WotR captures the spirit of Tolkien's world accurately and tastefully so. It is the perfect balance of dice battles and card play with thematic connections throughout. There are quite a few heavy systems working together on this one, which can also bog down the game - but it's as full an experience as you're going to get when it comes to a hundred plastic pieces on a board moving around and battling for who reigns supreme!

RATING: 10
[b][i]War of the Ring[/i][/b] is a rare combination of great theme with great mechanics that work with the theme. 

Allow me to give a sampling of the decisions in a round of War of the Ring. The core mechanic is the action dice that you roll every round. The dice tell you what you can do this round, and since you only have at most 10 dice it might seem like you don't have that many options. In truth, there are many possibilities (and this can paralyze newer players). For example, suppose you rolled 3 army results. Those dice must be used to mobilize armies on the board, but there are so many choices on where to move. Are you going to use those dice to reinforce/assault the men in Minas Tirith? The men of Gondor are strong, but conquering Gondor puts the Shadow half-way to victory. Or should you help/attack the elves of Lothlorien? It's a single stronghold, but shadow control of Lothlorien is an ideal staging area for moving in many directions and if you take it early you can hopefully interfere with the Fellowship movement. Should you wait until the end of the round when your opponent is committed to a strategy or should you perform your moves immediately before your opponent has time to adjust his strategy? And what about your newly drawn Rohan-based action cards, are these powerful enough to switch your attention to the horse lords? Meanwhile, what about your opponent? Should you set the pace of the game through aggression or should you only react to their actions? These aren't easy decisions, but they are fun.

Behind the military deployment is the Fellowship progression which acts as a game clock which the players partially control. The variable game clock is clever game design, and it's seamlessly integrated into the theme. So well is the theme integrated into this game that you don't even think of it as a game mechanism, but rather as the Fellowship marching towards Mordor. If you can imagine the Witch-King proclaiming "Do you not know Death when you see it?" as he smashes Rivendell to put the Shadow within an inch of victory while the Free Peoples hurry the Fellowship through Mordor as they throw caution to the wind, then you should try this game. 

[b]First Edition[/b]
The 1st edition is nearly identical to the 2e. The biggest change is a re-working of the cards for better balance in the 2e. Thankfully, Ares Games published an Upgrade Kit to help out 1e owners.

◊s,2e,tin◊

If I could give this game a thematic rating, it would be a 10. It totally captures the JRR Tolkien universe and will give you a new appreciation of the overall events of the LotR storyline!

The components are AMAZING! (even the non collectors edition which I have)

However, and I may be against the majority...the rules are horribly fiddly. There is so much going on...and you never really feel like you have tons of control. Armies move very slowly. Because of this, it drags a lot. The map is cramped...especially with the figures on it. The battle system is basically just Risk...with the option to add card bonuses. Even though, I think it's awesome how they have rules for the Fellowship movement that represent the dangers of the story very very well, the rules for that are also fiddly. 

Last game I played took almost 4 hours. Neither of us wanted to play afterwards...whereas my opponent and I could play Twilight Struggle and not feel that the 4 hours was as draining.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.9
"Guerra del Anillo" es sin lugar a dudas un magnifico juego de estrategia. Destaca la calidad de los componentes, sobre todo el precioso mapa de la Tierra Media y las estupendas miniaturas.

Pero mas alla de esa cuidada presentacion, que tambien podemos encontrar en otros juegos con una mecanica semejante a la del "Risk", estamos ante un verdadero juego de estrategia. Los disenadores han logrado transmitir el sabor de "El Senor de los Anillos" al juego, y han creado un reglamento que, sin ser demasiado complicado, logra que el juego tenga una notable profundidad.

A destacar, sin duda, la elegante forma en que se ha resuelto el viaje de la Compania del Anillo hacia el Monte del Destino, y la interaccion de los Companeros que la van abandonando (Gandalf, Trancos, Gimli, Legolas...) con la diplomacia y las operaciones militares.

Las cartas son otro gran hallazgo, ya que contribuyen a dar sabor a cada partida, y tienen un doble uso, como eventos o como cartas de combate.

En definitiva: un gran juego que merece la pena, y que no resulta tan caro en comparacion con otros juegos de estrategia, teniendo en cuenta la calidad y cantidad de componentes. El unico "pero" puede ser la duracion de una partida: hacen falta entre 4 y 5 horas para terminar una. Si eres fan de "El Senor de los Anillos", y si te gustan los juegos de estrategia o quieres iniciarte en ellos, "Guerra del Anillo" es un titulo imprescindible.

RATING: 7
Wow, the passion this game has solicited from all quarters of BGG is amazing.  From fights over font size to shills and even comments on nationalism!?.  As one person said, quoting Shakespeare, "The game's the thing."  

Yes, some have real concerns over hype, peice and area size, and a high luck factor, but the truth of the matter is that this is a highly playable game with replay value.  It's a 7 as I will play this again, but not anytime, and thus a good ranking is warrented; but it's by no means a 8 or 9.  If you are a europlayer unused to 2+ hour games, this is hardcore.  The luck factor may turn you off as well, but there are so many hard decisions that need to be made at every turn that you may find more resemblance to the familiar.  Two bonuses that make this a clear winner in purchasing at full price are: 1) it's a Lord of the Rings game that is beyond the spin-off genre, it's a strong medium war game; 2) you get a bonus mini hobby as you really need to paint the figs.  Take a serious look at some of the images and see how much love there is for the genre.  Great game, lots of passion, buy it... when all is said and done, the game really is the thing!

BUT BEWARE if you paint the figs and they suck (subjective as that may be), you may find it hard to sell if unsatisfied by the game play.

RATING: 5
A fun game which reminds me slightly of the olden days of Risk and Axis and Allies. A GIANT board, with hundreds of minis, along with in-depth gameplay,  and the theme really sold this one for me. A good war-game when you and a buddy want to sit down and decide the fate of the One Ring.

Slight imbalances could ruin your good time, but I guess these are fixed with the expansion. It's long set up and tear down, have it seeing very little time off the shelf. The production is great, but the figures (unpainted) are hard to tell apart, and having four armies all the same colour make it a virtual nightmare to sort.

But, if you're a LotR fan, and/or a wargamer, you'll likely enjoy this one. I found it to be dry, and far to fiddly for what it's trying to accomplish.

RATING: 8
I did not expect to like War of the Ring. When I first read about the project, several warning bells were triggered in my brain: games with miniatures, licensed games, games marketed at teenagers, fantasy wargames, games designed by a "design team," hodgepodge hybrid games in the tradition of Fantasy Flight or Games Workshop, games certain to be overridden with errata, games likely to have all sorts of cumbersome rules exceptions to stay true to the theme. On top of all that, there was already a game called "War of the Ring," and it was a complete mess by almost all accounts. If ever there was a game that I was predisposed to hate, this was it. 

Well, you've already guessed what comes next. I gave it a shot, and I loved it. I'm not convinced it's a game that I'll be playing years from now, but after five plays I'm still excited about playing again. That's good replayability for a game of this length. I would probably have played it several more times already if my main wargame partner liked it as well as I do. Designing a game like this has got to be an extremely tough task, and it's hard for me to imagine the design team doing a better job. The action die system works wonderfully, the cards are good, the war vs. fellowship tension is balanced well, and there are multiple strategic paths – sometimes. I would have liked a bit more flexibility on that last point. Often your strategy is stereotyped by your current card selection or your action dice or the sheer length of time it takes your troops to get to the more remote parts of the board. This is the primary reason I don’t foresee War of the Ring standing up to heavy play over the course of several years. But how many games of this sort really earn that many plays? Very, very few. Overall, War of the Ring succeeds in the dual task of providing an exciting, interesting game and evoking the events and atmosphere of the Lord of the Rings.

RATING: 6
I want to like this game. In fact there are things about this game that I do like. I'm a big LOTR fan and this game oozes theme. I seem to be becoming more partial to wargames so that aspect of the game appeals to me also. I do want to play this again because, for the most part, I enjoy the experience of playing.
However, for a game that lasts around 3 hours it seems like there is far too much luck involved. In the two games I've played it's basically come down to a crap shoot as to whether good or bad tiles are drawn from the hunt tile bag once the fellowship reaches Mordor. Both times I played as the Shadow side, the first time I won due to corruption of the Fellowship, the second time I lost as they made it to Mount Doom. Both times left me feeling dissatisfied with the result though as it seemed like the previous 3 hours were a waste of time and we could have just skipped to drawing tiles to determine the winner.
Throw in the fact that bad dice rolling for Action Event Die can leave you with very few options, and bad dice rolling in combat can mean strong positions can rapidly turn to crud, and it just leaves a bit of a sour taste in my mouth.
There's almost a great game here but it just doesn't quite work for me.

RATING: 7
balancing the war versus the fellowship is a great concept.  excellent theme and design.  game has dropped in my ratings for sitting on the shelf while other games get played; it seems that every game develops along pretty much the same line (clash at Helm's Deep followed by clash at Minas Tirith).  I think the low number of cards per turn, combined with the action dice and low movement rates tends to restrict the amount of viable tactics; you pretty much always know what someone is up to.

Repeated play reveals that the path of the fellowship is the only real element that matters; Sauron will capture the necessary VP, so unless the fellowship moves fast and lucky, it's all over.

That being said, it is fun to play once every year or two for the story and tension.  Just don't take it too seriously.

includes the 2nd edition upgrade kit and Lords of Middle-Earth expansion.
