RATING: 3
I've read the books, loved the movies, am a big fantasy fan, and love detailed plastic miniatures as well. So I really wanted to like this game, but...

Setup is a pain. Many different miniatures look all the same and I can't understand why they don't come in different colors. The map is horrible to read and gets cluttered already in the setup. Playability suffers from the fidelity to Middle-Earth, as some distant regions are not even worth to bother with. Fiddly rules. Very static and slow gameplay. Cards are very unbalanced, and a lot of them are only useful in very specific situations; too many times you no longer have any chance of using the cards when you draw them. The cards intend to bring some flavor of the story, but without them, the game would probably be twice as fast. Luck fest. No fun.

I have to confess I'm no wargamer, but I own and enjoy a few light wargames, such as "Star Wars: The Queen's Gambit", which also may take long (but is a much better game), and "Nexus Ops", which provides some good light fun.

Muy buen juego con una cuidada ambientacion y estetica muy del agrado para todo fan de la obra de JRR Tolkien. 

La larga preparacion y las mecanicas algo farragosas dificultan a los jugadores meterse en la partida y tener una buena impresion del juego. En mi opinion una vez superadas estas, Guerra del Anillo consigue su objetivo de reflejar el arco argumental y el desarrollo de la historia de manera analoga al libro y peliculas de "el señor de los anillos" pero por desarrollo y mecanicas queda un poco desmejorado frente a otros juegos de guerra o de aventura. 

Juego al que ayuda mucho disponer de una guia clarificada de las mecanicas de juego. (sin ellas, a pesar de su calidad en materiales, le hubiera cascado un señor 7)

RATING: 8
A great game which ties very closely to the book in terms of events without removing player choice. It is not a hard game to learn (despite the bloated rule book, we use the first rule) but there is a lot of depth and tension as the ring approaches mount Doom. Despite being highly asymmetric I found it very well balanced, the majority of our games get down to the wire and when they do not it is due to a series of errors or good play. 

If you like LoTR you are very likely to enjoy this. The second edition nicely fixed some issues with the first. I got the upgrade cards which were well worth it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Gives an excellent feeling of the LotR theme without forcing your hand or too much rules overhead. Very simple things lead you to same types of decisions that the characters in the books have to make. The layout of the areas on the board is excellent, Rivendell feels like an inaccesible dell tucked away, Moria is the quickest but riskiest route to Morodor although the gap of Isengard is tempting too, Emyn Muil feels blasted and barren. The splitting off of companions linked to the reduction in hunt dice, along with the companions combat abilities isn't an overwhelming push to chuck out everyone but Sam and keeping everyone on board is viable. The combat mechanics feel a bit retro but are servicable and quick. The setup of pieces scattered all through middle earth is good, it makes it more than just a case of lining up on either side and smashing into each other, evil can quickly pose a threat to most areas on the board.

The not so great stuff: The good side is more fun to play. You have more to do, where the evil side is pretty much just racing to smash strongholds. The evil player can win just by the good player crapping up on luck, which feels a bit anticlimatic. It is almost impossible to tell the elves/Gondor/Rohan pieces apart from each other. 

As a side note, the Quantum Fellowship rules are brilliant. The faster they move, the less sure you are of their position. Their position is probabilistic until observed. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Certainly a HUGE improvement over the SPI version, and probably the best depiction of the entire epic war on a board game yet designed. I wish some of the rules could be a little streamlined, though. Like why does a character die move EITHER the Fellowship OR all the other characters? Why not simplify, and have a character die move EVERYBODY or ONE? Wouldn't that be easier to remember? And when is it that a country is activated as opposed to moved on the political track? Can't remember if it's the first time an army is attacked, or when their zone is entered, or a fort captured, or...ug. Little things like that. And the luck dimension is fairly significant. More about allocating dice than anything else. Still, all in all, it's good.

RATING: 10
Great game, but probably a bit over-hyped as it does have some flaws.  Its a great design and covers the subject matter beautifully.  The components are top notch.  This would be a 10 if the designers would have tried a little harder to make it easy to play.  Its a risk-esque combat system where you roll trying to get 5s to hit.  That sort of game should be easy to figure out straight out of the box.  This game isn't.  If it gets reprinted with some of the niggling issues fixed, this could be a 9.5 easily.


Really fantastic game with a lot of strategic depth.  The rules aren't as bad as people make them out to be, but in places they seem overly complicated for what the results are (for example, the political rules for activating) The icons tell you nothing, the pieces are more difficult to tell apart than they need to be, and there is some gameyness to some of the decision making because of the countermix.  (Example:  too few elf regulars)                                                                                Also, it seems with the super-simple combat rules, it wouldn't have been too difficult to differentiate the units somehow?  Why can't elves get some bonus in forest, dwarves in mountains, etc.  Also, many of the fellowship members special abilities seem very bland.  They could have been sexed up a bit, which would have made a FP victory more possible.  Hopefully the expansion will fix these problems.          

 I also think its too long for what it is, mostly because you know who is going to win part way through in some games.  The action dice seemed neat at first, but really don't constrain your choices that much, and when they do you feel like you've been cheated by lady luck.  Still, a beautiful game thats a lot of fun.  I'll definitely pick up the expansion at some point.                                                                    
                                             Don't get me wrong!!!  This game is tons of fun, and deserves every bit of the  rating I give it.  No CRT, plays fast for a wargame of this scale, and the card and dice combination keeps things interesting.  The pieces are all absolutely gorgeous and I could see myself playing this game every day for a couple of weeks without getting bored.  I agree with folks that say that the icons on the board and bits are impossible to decipher, but I think in a way it adds to the charm of the game.  The game adds for a lot of different strategies on both sides which is a huge plus for a 2 player wargame that isn't based on scenarios.

UPDATE:  In anticipation of this thing getting redone (likely in 2008) with the new parts from the expansion, painted minis, and probably some other new stuff thrown in, I've traded this game.  I'm excited about getting the deluxe edition with the expansion stuff in it.  My next step will be getting a signed "Bill the Pony" card from Kris Hall.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
[Initial Impression: 3]
I love this game...I hate this game...this game scares me!  Huge, expansive, truly epic, all as a Tolkien wargame should be.  There are a lot of fiddly issues with the sameness of the figures (confusing), the fact that a massed army won't fit on the board, that you're constantly looking up card effects and rules (admittedly a first-play issue), and with the dice determining your actions.  But the real death-blow was that, after all that set up, after the in-depth rules reading, after *6 hours*...the remaining members of the Fellowship had infiltrated Mordor, were climbing Mt. Doom and the heat of the fires was rising, just 3 spaces from victory and only about half-way along the corruption track...when a random die roll resulted in a random tile draw, resulting in another random die roll which resulted in an overpowering, hopes-dashing, game-ending 6 added corruption (nothing else in the game can add such a massive amount of corruption at once).  Any game that allows a day-long endeavor to end without regard for strategy (the Sauron player was being far too conservative with his forces and the Fellowship entered Mordor with almost no corruption) is simply hateful.  I sometimes feel like I want to play again, but then the fear grips me...I just haven't had the heart or willingness to tempt fate to get it back on the table. A first experience that may have ruined the game for me.  Ask me to play and re-rate it again in a year, when my pain has faded...

RATING: 5.1
Some good concepts here (the activation dice rolls, political mechanic to get allies into the war, and the different methods of winning), but, serious questions about game balance (too easy for the SP to gain a military victory and too difficult for FP), the are cards too imbalanced and contain too many references for those us that don't live by the fellowship. Also, difficult to understand the ring mechanic (hunt, revealed, located, etc).

I don't understand the hype on this one; other than the theme of course. 7th ranked wargame? Not in the realm of any real wargamers I know.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is superior to the 1978 SPI wargame of the same name or ICE's 1984 Fellowship of the Ring. Seems easy to grasp and straight-forward until you play. The cards drive your opportunities, so you can't go into a game determined to play out a certain strategy -- you must adapt to the changing circumstances dictated by the cards in your hand. The dice determine what activities are possible in your turn -- a wonderful idea for limiting command and control potential. Very subtle, very true to the books. Lots of replay potential. Searching for this game lead to my stumbling upon BGG, for which it will always have a spot in my heart.

RATING: 7
It's a physically big game, with tons of figures and a big board.  However, too many of the areas on the board are too small to hold more than 5 or 6 units.  The result is that you have to keep your armies off the board and use tokens instead.  Too many of the units look like each other and the ringbearer can be hard to catch if on their own.  There's nothing in the game that encourages attempts to follow the storyline from the book and it can all come down to some big battle in the middle of the board.

However, there's quite a good simple battle game here, with some very nice components.  Ignore the book and work with what you have and it's an asymmetric skirmish of puny good overcoming rampant evil.

The dice are probably the best part of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Nice, if underdeveloped, Axis & Allies style game on the middle two-thirds of Tolkien's Lord of the Rings (from the departure from Rivendell to the climax at Mount Doom).  Nice components and some clever game systems promote a pleasant playing experience, but even as someone who has never read Tolkien, I found the game's narrative lacking.  The book featured climactic battles at places like Helm's Deep and Minas Tirith, but in the game the Shadow armies are so overpowered that they just walk over the Free Peoples with ease.  This isn't helped by the combat cards, whose effects largely negate the defensive bonus afforded to the Free Peoples for defending in such places.  So the end result is just a race game:  the FP heads the ring to Mordor, and the Shadow blitzkriegs Middle Earth.  Both the narrative and the gameplay would have been improved had the Shadow forces been toned down.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.7
Es largo (3-4 h) y complicado… pero no se puede esperar menos de un juego épico que sigue fiel a las novelas, pero que a su vez es muy flexible al dejar el final abierto. Juego tenso y reglas bastante liosas… No te fies: es un wargame (ligero, pero wargame)… pero no es sólo un wargame. Tiene algo de los Card Driven Games (CDG) como puede ser el uso variado de una misma carta o el concepto de “activación”.  Buena mecánica la de la “caza” y la “corrupción de Frodo”. Pegas: no es intuitivo al haber muchas excepciones en las reglas, fallo de diseño gráfico en el tablero (las miniaturas no caben en algunas regiones), algunas miniaturas se confunden. Sobreproducido. La suerte incide en este juego más que en otros. Hay que ser tolkeniano para que te guste. Sólo para 2 jugadores, para 4 es un "apaño" muy mal hecho.

BAja de 8.9 a 8.7 en OCT 2013 porque no lo juego tanto

RATING: 9
If it weren't for the ridiculous playing time, I would be inclined to give this game a 10.  The tension and suspense is absolutely killer.  The action die system is a great idea and makes it so the game doesn't bog down into redundancy.  I'm truly surprised by the balance through playtesting the designers managed to achieve given the very, very different feels for each side.  My friend owns this and I don't mind setting aside the time every now and again, but this sort of epic really demands a lot from a person.  This is probably the single reason I won't be purchasing it (aside from finding another willing individual to commit as well).   Still don't mind giving it a glowing review though.

Update:  It's become somewhat apparent that the Fellowship needs a significant amount of luck to overcome a moderately competent shadow player.  I'll have to play a few more games to confirm my suspicions on play balance before I lower my rating.

Re-Update:  Yeah so I bought it (couldn't refuse the price I was given).  I also want to retract some of my concerns over balance.  I believe I've been strongly overvaluing rushing the Fellowship instead of breaking off companions and sticking to safe free settlement travel.  I'm really tempted to bump the rating up to a ten, but I need to see some successful Fellowship strategies first...

RATING: 8
While I doubt that the power of this game will corrupt my soul and slowly warp my physical appearance, I do see what the hype is all about.  One can quibble with some of the choices in the physical production -- why or why only 2 colors of plastic?  The map is beautiful, but a little more functionality would have been nice.  The iconography has to be memorized, and is thus also less useful than something a little less stylistic and more functional.  

That said, there is a TON going on here.  I can't imagine how many games I would need to play before I was sure I had pursued all possible strategies.  But it would be easily in the dozens, maybe the hundreds.  This is not a light game, and don't even think it is particularly light for a wargame, but it is tense and seems to move fast even though it probably chalks up at 3+ hrs.  I'll be playing again, but given the playtime and setup, not as often as I would like.

RATING: N/A
Juego basado en el señor de los anillos. Cada jugador lleva un bando. Tira una serie de dados especiales que le permitirá realizar una serie de acciones. Crear ejercitos, moverlos, combatir, asedios, movimiento de la compañia, bastante majo
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.25
If interested in WOTR, consider the following very carefully:

 - The rulebook is over 20 pages
 - The FAQ is 15 pages
 - There are 500 threads of people asking questions about the rules

Okay, still interested? 

 - It is a 2 - 4 hour game with quite a good dose of luck and yes the whole game can come down to one tile pull.

There, you should be scared off now (I know the tile pull ending sure wasn't too appealing for me). Well if not then like me you are probably LOTR obsessed or just so damn intrigued on why everyone loves this game. 

The answer is simple really, the production values are absolutely stunning and the gameplay is remarkable close to the movies/books. This realism comes at a HUGE price of complexity however and very large time investment in the beginning but WOW is it ever cool. I'm sure if not for the theme this would not make it over an 8 for me, the same rating I give Axis & Allies. 

In some ways this is very similiar to A&A for me:

 - Realism at the price of complexity
 - A lot of rules making for big time investment starting off
 - Dice for combat (luck factor)
 - Movement rules similiar
 - Attack/Retreat rules similiar
 - Longer than usual game
 - Room for different strategies
 - Very fun to play

Of course this is mostly general stuff and gameplay does feel completely different from A&A for the most part. You have all the special characters with their abilities, special cards with abilities that can usually be used every turn and in combat, dice to determine your actions, 4 different victory conditions, activation rules, etc. 

Not since A&A have I had to read the rules more than once for more than 6 hours which is why I highly recommend NOT learning this by yourself like I did, a terrible way to learn a game like this. Even DOA my fav game had less learning curve and rules. The rules do follow the law of logic thankfully and are mostly clear but I do not like how they were put together. I'm not sure that 'less would be more' here as all the details really add to the game so I think the complexity is necessary and in the end, worth it. With a nice turn summary and more play the game is starting to flow now and we are enjoying playing out the story of the ring very much. This will most likely be my 3rd "10" rating after more play but we'll see depending on replay, luck etc, could even go down a bit.

Bottom line: If you want to play out the story in 30 minutes with very little rules than LOTR-TC is your game. If you want the 'real deal' and know what you are getting into with this then the payoff should be worth it, it is a gaming masterpiece that a lot of work went into to have the WOTR come beautifully to life!

RATING: 7.3
I really enjoy playing this. The comparisons being drawn to Risk, Memoir 44, and Battle Cry are all valid in a certain sense. The only reason it's not a 10 is that my Nazgul won't stand straight! The battle are primarily dice rolls with modifications from characters, cards, and the number of attackers/defenders. This is much more advanced than Risk and much better than Memoir'44 and Battle Cry. There is much more detail and the theme is very rich and brought to life with the cards and characters. I recommend this to anyone who isn't afraid of some dice rolling and who enjoys the LOTR theme. I'd say this falls in the grey catagory between Euro game and war game, with other great games such as Wallenstein and Game of Thrones though probably more on par with Axis & Allies but more advanced.

Initial Rating: 6.5 (April 2011)

To be perfectly honest. I don’t remember much about the game, other than the disappointment I felt when I played it. The thing is, War if the Ring was/is very well ranked and regarded and it was the favorite game of the friend who explained it to me, however, I remember I didn’t like the game very much and on 2011 I liked most games I played. Perhaps it ws over hyped.

It is too slow and too long for what it offers. Best with 2 players.

I remember I thought the game was a lot harder for the Free People player.

The game components are very well done.

Bottom line, I have changed a lot since 2011. Perhaps now I can appreciate better the game, but my desire to play it again is low so I’m not sure if I will ever play it again. If I do, I will come back to write a proper comment.

RATING: 9.5
Yes there's a lot of randomness, yes there's a bunch of rules (which you pick up fairly easily after playing one or two games), yes the board's too small (even though it's huge) and the pieces should have been different colors, but I love this game anyway.  There's only two sides, so there no annoying kingmaking that these types of games tend to have.  The game captures the theme so well that I don't mind that I can't roll 5's or 6's to save my life.

After just a few games, you can set-up and play this in 2-3 hrs (maybe less) as long as the Fellowship is moved aggressively.  I prefer it this way.  I would hate to play this for 4+ hours only to run into a bad sting of hunt tile draws in Mordor.

Update after more plays.  I still love how this game brings the book to life in it's mechanics.  The FP need to move aggressively to have a chance at winning.  We can now set-up and play in about 1.5 hours.

RATING: 9
A great strategy boardgame in Tolkien's world. Every game is different, the game is faithful to the theme of the books, game balance is near-perfect and the strategic options are wide. The game does an awesome job of transmitting Tolkien's world to the players. Being a Tolkien fan helps - my rating would be 1-2 points lower if I wasn't one - but is not compulsory. This is a good game for anybody interested in getting an asymmetric wargame that is still perfectly balanced, and which varies a great deal from one play to another. The single downside is having to know the rules pretty well in order to enjoy the game - but it is really worth it (complexity is the only thing that keeps this game from being a full 10 in my books - it does turn some people off, and therefore makes the times I'm able to enjoy this game not as numerous as I'd hope).

RATING: 10
Stunning, epic affair that captures the Lord of the Rings theme in a manner I doubt anyone imagined could be done. The Shadow's military might and action dice advantage (which can be swiftly increased by bringing out Saruman) are set against the Free People's resiliency to sieges and, of course, the Fellowship's movement towards Mordor. The action cards lend a wonderful boost to each game's unique story arc and can spring a number of nasty surprises on an opponent in regular play or when used for combat effects. 

There are some issues: the map is a drab, disappointing reddish-brown with weird symbology for VP spaces and three different settlement icons that can be tough to distinguish. Nation borders are tricky in spots, too. The action card text is almost impossibly small in many cases, bordering on microsopic. The Free figures can look a bit samey, but I ended up painting the bases of mine to lend a hand visually. The rules as written are a catastrophe and result in many people playing key rules wrong the first few times out--definitely print out a player aid or two from this site. 

Fortunately, none of those things come anywhere close to knocking this down from a flat-out perfect 10. A game I will never, ever turn down.

RATING: 9.1
Where to begin?  Great board, theme, design, everything.  Rich rich rich theme.  Addictive and, to a large extent, unique - the mechanics have not really been copied anywhere, and they work extremely well.  This is NOT just Risk with LOTR pasted over - people who claim this have clearly never played the game (plus Risk is essentially a negotiation game and this is not).  Once you know the rules it flows well and plays fast - the game will seem to finish quickly!  Only gripe is that luck plays a bit too big of a role, in the sense that there are often key moments where a single good/bad dice roll or card draw will heavily influence the outcome.  But there IS strategy and the better player will win more often.  I will still play many more times because it is no doubt a very fun game.

RATING: 6
While it captures the feel of the books/movies and seems to have a nice narrative to it (makes you feel like you are writing your own version of the story while playing it), I feel the dice system seems to slow this game down way too much. Your actions are determined by the roll of the dice which bogs down your strategy. It can take a while for reinforements to come in needlessly. I feel if you take away the dice and implement an action system, the game would play out quicker (takes too long as it is) and give you more control over your strategy without taking away from the thematic feel of the game. Also has a long set up time. But there are very good game mechanics there that capture the theme of the stories.

RATING: 6
After playing Middle-Earth-Quest I downgraded this game to 6/10 as in direct comparison I rather play MEQ for a Tolkien fix as it is more approachable and plays more relaxing.
---
Contrary to the stats here on the geek, the Free People won in my first two games. In the second game we were able to shorten the playing time from 5 to 4 hours. The easy way to spoil the game is to read and execute the available strategy guides. If you just neglect these, there should be enough possiblities for the average gamer (i.e. somebody playing this every once in a while with months in between) to never get fed up with this game.
Don't play this game with non-gamers. They likely won't get it. The game is too open which will stall their decisions. 6.5 hard hours were enough lesson to me. Game ended with corruption victory of Sauron.
After my second four-player-game I think I enjoy this more with four than only with two players. On the other side, I enjoy this game a huge bit less as a whole because of two fucking die rolls messing up the win after five hours (chances for the result which led to the loss were 1:81!!!).

RATING: 7
Having only played the introductory game, I consider my rating to be very volatile at this point, but likely to move up.  I wouldn't want to play this all the time, but it is definitely quite fun.  I'm not a Tolkien expert, but the game has a lot of the flavor of the story and feels very thematic.  There is a pretty big learning curve that I have yet to fully conquer.  The shortcomings of the game all have to do with appearance.  The map is confusing, having to scour the board to find things, or distinguish between towns and cities.  The pieces are fine I guess (I'm no fantasy fan), but the Nazguls tend to fall over quite easily, and they're too big for the space they're supposed to occupy.  The cards could be designed much better as well, for easier readability.  However, that said, I enjoy the game and look forward to becoming more familiar with it.

Traded away - just too many rules to realistically expect to play it that much.

RATING: 7
A very solid game with a lot of flavor.  The rulebook is very poorly organized and the Event cards, while injecting a lot of excitement in the game, are occasionally fiddly.  The game gently shepherds the players along the path followed by the actual parties in the books, but offers enough opportunity for creativity to create plausible and interesting alternative histories.  For me, it falls short of a couple of the criterion for a "perfect" Tolkien game -- for one, the hunt (while a great mechanic) is passive rather than active.  I have a few other quibbles like this, but mostly, I think it's a very solid game.

RATING: 7
Rated on the basis of one very long game. I enjoyed it a lot, and the thought and effort that went into balancing it yielded good results: when I played, I was Sauron and my friend was on *the last space* of the Crack of Doom when I managed to increase his corruption enough to win. The entire game came down to a roll of the die: 1-3, he won, 4-6 I did. I rolled a 6. Insane! And also a little annoying. It made me feel like I could've flipped a coin and saved myself eight hours. But then again, the production value is so high that the game was well worth it. Unlike a lot of more schematic Tolkien games, you aren't following the story exactly, but creating the War of the Ring as you go. The game leans toward conquest of Rohan and Gondor, but they aren't necessary, and there are plenty of ways the Eye can win by, for example, attacking to the north, or by sitting back, not mustering much at all, and hunting for the Ring. The range of choices you have per turn is satisfying, and the action dice introduce enough randomness that you will always get PART of what you want to do, but rarely will you get the exact results that you need. I really like this game, and I'm curious to see over the next few plays how much strategy has to do with the outcome. [ETA: Played again, this time as Free Peoples; got trounced. So much for too much balance!]

Yet another update. I'm rerating this from 9 to 7 just because I've played it so much I'm sick to death of it. I still think it's phenomenally well balanced and designed, but there are a fair number of fiddly rules to keep track of and a lot of winning strategies. The trick is to figure out which route your opponent is going to take, and optimize your own strategy to take advantage. Is Sauron sending all his Nazgul to Minas Tirith? Then start moving Frodo and the gang. Is Saruman in the game early? Then get Aragorn or Gandalf down to Rohan and try to hold the line, getting the Shadow to spend dice. So much of the game is a defensive maneuver for the Free Peoples. One thing I did wrong my first three or four games of this was to underestimate the power of splitting off Fellowship members to accelerate movement on the diplo track.

RATING: 8

This leads to players having to understand their strengths and weaknesses, and then making informed choices about how to use them. I want to play this more and more to better explore the system and its options!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
War of the Ring is, by far, the most immersive game I've played: every mechanism is there so that you can recreate and/or rewrite the Lord of the Rings history. If you like the films you should give it a try, but if you consider yourself a fan of LotR and haven't played it yet, put it in the first place of your "to do" list.

The game itself is quite a challenge: both sides are completely different and, not only require a different strategies, but also offer completely different game experiences. 

As the free peoples you see how armies of orcs start to gather and crush everything that gets in their way. You can't stop them, but you have to do your best to slow them as much as possible. Your focus is on buying Frodo and his ever-shrinking fellowship enough time to reach mount doom and destroy the ring.

As the shadow armies you feel completely overpowered: you have enough power and brute force to crush everything that gets in your way. You have the potential of crushing everything, but... Will you stomp your foes fast enough? How much will you focus on pure stompin' and how much will you focus on catching those pesky hobbits?

Regarding tactics and strategy, luck has its impact, but it can be controlled. On the one hand, each card has two actions, and can be used either in combat or to get some sort of benefit in the board positioning. The more chrome-heavy actions are usually tagged along with ones of general use, so it's weird for a card to feel useless. On the other hand, some people complain that the game is too centred on dice, as in a game as thematic as this one, dice do add a lot of tension and drama.

All in all, a perfect game for hardcore Tolkien fans, as well as for those who simply enjoy the films and like highly thematic games.

RATING: 3
Two-player wargame. Needlessly complicated rulebook and although the miniatures are nice, the difference between the different races is barely noticeable (why no different colors?). Basically an advanced Risk where everything is dictated by dice (dice for the actions you can take during a turn and dice for combat - don't really mind the last, but having the possible actions detemined by dice can be very frustrating). All in all, this is an epic game which takes ages to finish and gets tedious after a while. So, I'd rather spend that time watching the three extended DVD's of Lord of the Rings (again :-)).

RATING: 6
It is a very nice game. It's complex, probably to complex, because players can be easily overwhelmed by rules, but playing gives a lot of fun.

The concept of two-area conflict is very good. One of the areas is military, the second is quest for the Ring. The Shadow player is military crushing, but he has only limited time to destroy the Free People player and he must divide his attention to the Fellowship as well. Very, very nice.

The only huge drawback of the game is, as in all FF games, the randomizes both in battles and, especially, at the final stage of the Quest. Because of the separate decks it is not inconvenient in card arrangement.

I could comment the game flow using only one sentence: "We are playing and after that we are drawing 5 tiles to see is the winner..."

RATING: 4
I'm pretty close to giving up on this game.  I'll give it another try or two, but most of the people I've played it with haven't wanted to play it again.

I don't mind having to deal with adversity, but it seems like there's a little [i]too[/i] much of that here.  In my last play, I decided I wanted to try to run the fellowship as fast as possible, and then had turns where I rolled all helmets when there was one die in the hunt pool, and all swords when there were four or five dice in the pool.  Shrug... OK, game, why don't [i]you[/i] play, and let me know how it went.

I'm not crazy about the effects of the cards; they feel gamey & unrealistic to me.  Maybe I'd like it better if there were ways for the opponent to cancel events as they were played, so that they were less of a sure thing?  Knowing that the Dead Men of Dunharrow will show up if you get Strider across the continent may help you recreate the books, but from the point of view of the fellowship or the leaders in the game, what does it mean to be holding that card in your hand?  "Ooh, if I have a Hobbit in a battle, I'll get this bonus"--nobody in the game world would think that.  (Compare to something like "if I move troops to this region, maybe I can slow the enemy's advance.")  Similarly, the choice between the event and the combat effect on most cards is not one which would ever face a participant in the war.

I really hate the rulebook, both for the irrelevant blather which just gets in the way when you're looking for a rule--put that in a sidebar, people!--and for how often the rule you're looking for is four pages away from where you expect to find it.

Update: traded to MScrivner 2/26/08.

RATING: 5
Just one play behind 4-player. The game is random enough for being this long already, and with two Shadow Peoples "factions" you have somewhat limited info with the SP side's event cards. But I see a lot of comments about the game being lopsided to the SP so maybe 2 SP players works as a fix.
The theme is implemented nicely, but the game pretty much goes on rails. There's no real incentive for the Free Peoples player to go for the conquest victory, and the SP has to get very very lucky to win by corruption. So the strategies are quite set from the beginning, and the tactical options are quite dictated by the action dice and card draws. While pretty lukewarm, I want to try it again. If it would settle down to less than 2 hours with further plays, it could be an ok beer and pretzels distraction every once in a while.

RATING: 8
I like how this game seems to stay true to the Lord of the Rings trilogy. It is almost impossible for the free peoples to win with a military victory, and I like the fellowship component of the game. Thats not to say the game is scripted, because I don't feel that it is - but for a simulation of the events in the LotR trilogy, War of the Ring does a damn fine job.

It's interesting to see how little dice the Shadow player allocates to the hunt early on, only to see him allocating as many as possible when the Ringbearers get too close to Mt. Doom.

I enjoy the suspense of the game when the fellowship makes it to the Mt. Doom track.

Likewise the battles at key points such as Minas Tirith and Helms Deep are exciting, and the Free Peoples player has his work cut out for him.

Part of the appeal of this game for me is really the content, or the theme of the game, however, I find that the mechanics of the game work perfectly to compliment the theme and to recreate, in boardgame form, the dual struggle of the War of the Ring and the quest to destroy the actual One Ring.

This is an example where the theme of the game combined with excellent mechanics makes for a very satisfying game.

The more think about it the more I enjoy this game and come to appreciate it. I think this is probably the definitive LotR game.

The rulebook could be clearer - and I think the "Hunt" should be included earlier in the rulebook then where it is (at the end) because really I think it is the most important part of the game.

My most recent play confirms that I don't care for the rulebook, as it's still hard for me to pinpoint a specific rule, but i'm getting better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Very thematic and immersive game which is quite compelling in the story arc of the game.  There are many times the Free Peoples will feel overwhelmed by the forces of Sauron and wonder if there is really any hope and then find hope in the Fellowship as they slowly make their way up Mt. Doom.  There is difficulty in discerning who is who with some of the figures.  I wish they would have some marked bases to help differentiate between the different Free People nations in particular.  There is also not nearly enough space on the map in certain regions but there are counters made available to replace the plastic figures in those situations.  I do also think how they differentiated between elite and regular troops is quite clever as there are interesting decisions in some instances of what dies in battle...elite units live longer, but provide the same attack value as a regular.  The Nazguls constantly tripping over themselves was bothersome, but I must admit the sculpts were impressive and stand out on the board.  Dice were sweet as well.  Not sure why they didn't include 10 d6s for combat?..unless my copy was just shorted five dice.  First game will take a while and most likely I expect I would need to play several times to truly grok the game.  I think I would prefer to keep this two player, but 3-4 player brings in some other interesting decisions/difficulties.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Italian view on the Middle Earth.  The box is huge!  Wanting to play this game someday while not wanting to own it. There will probably a reprint in the future as the game is already OOP.  

The game is almost a perfect combination of traditional boardwargaming with miniatures - minus some of the areas with insufficient space which are not able to hold all the miniatures in one place when they move in to conquer the area.  Nonetheless, War of the Ring is the closest thing I have ever seen for simulating the story Lord of the Ring.    

We have a 4-player game but it seems to me 3-players game is the best as the Free Peoples can be controlled by the same person while the side has fewer action tiles than the Shadow Armies in a game turn.

For Tolkein's fans, the map is the most gorgeous thing one could probably dream of having to realize.  All the Middle Earth geography, names of regions and places are included on the map - a kind of authentic research into the Tolkien's world.  Although I am much less enthusiastic of the miniatures and figures, they do convey a visualization of being in Middle Earth reminiscent of the dark trilogy movies.  Overall, the game associates with the theme of the story very closely and it is not surprising to see it has a very high ranking in BGG with regards to theme. 

Before going to the good sides of the many game features I like, there are two minor quibbles.  The first is the size of the Event Card text is way too small...even for good eyes like me.  Many of the game rules and dependent variables require the reading of the cards often and the effects of the cards can be quite significant - whether a nation would join the war or raise new troops.  

The second thing is the some of the area size on the map are not sufficient enough to hold the maximum stacking of 10 units.  While the figurines are finely diecut and they look really lively and authentic to the description as was told by the book and the movie, particularly Nazgul the flying bombers, they appears to be crammed in a small cage shouting out for help. Well, I shouldn't be that picky as the map has to tell the Middle Earth in proportion, before you remember every small places or regions in the world.

Back to the game features, the Fellowship has a Guide when the Companions start with them.  The Companion may leave the group and go back to the home country to raise troops.  The Free People are in need of them AND they are in real short supply....while stock still lasts.  A Free People unit, when eliminated, is never able to recover to return.  The Shadow Army, however, can always raise as many troops as reinforcements from the dead pool.  Talking about the difference between walking dead and human flesh and blood.

The entire game system is designed based on the Action Dieroll. There are six different types of  actions. In the beginning of the game, each side will roll the action dices - the Free People have 4 but the Shadow Army have 7 (both can be increased to a certain extent upon entering of some characters).  What the dieroll turns up will dictate largely the kind of actions each side can do.  For example, to recruit new troops as mentioned above, you need a "Muster" dieroll.  To perform an action, you spend a die with the corresponding side up.  So the Free People is really restricted in resources while the Shadow Army is powerful to perform a lot of the actions and with more flexibility in strategy.

The Army (with or without Character leader) can move to the adjacent region one at a time. Two armies are activated to move with a banner while a single army with a Character is activated with a sword.  The sword dieroll can also move ALL (remember, it is all) companions Character not in the Fellowship or to move the Fellowship itself.  The Shadow player can move ALL Nazgul and Minions with this dieroll.  It creates the situation that the Companions should leave at the right time and go calling for help.  In a four player game, although the two players control units of some of the Nations only, they can actually move all the individual companions or Nazgul and Minions.  

I like the simplicity of the combat system where the number of combat dices is determined by the number of units in the Army, plus the number of possible dieroll due to the presence of leaders.  Each dieroll scores a hit on a 5 or 6. Combat cards and Characters special abilities can modify the number of dieroll via combat strength and leadership rating.  The attacker declares whether he is going to use a card first and then the defender.  The cards effects are resolved simultaneously though.  There is a good variety of cards text and effect.  They are pretty good at relating the events in the novel to the game in gaming terms.  I really like how the designer handles the card management aspect.  For each hit, it is satisfied by removing one Regular Unit or replace one Elite Unit with one Regular Unit.  So it means the Elite Units has two steps to hit. But then, an Elite Unit only grant you the same one combat die as a Regular Unit. The attacker can keep on attacking until he chooses to stop or the defender elects to retreat.  

Defending Units can retreat into an adjacent region or retreating into a stronghold if there is one in the embattled region.  A siege of the stronghold would start.  The attacker only hit on a 6 while the besieged hits on a 5 or 6.  The attacker can continue another round of siege only if he reduces an Elite Unit into a regular one.  Simple and effective siege rules.

No rule is complete in a game based on the Lord of the Ring if there is nothing about the "Ring" and the hunt for it.  The "Fellowship" comprised basically of Frodo and Sam.  They are bent on reaching Mount Doom to complete the "quest".  The Companions can stay with the group or leave to raise more Free People troops.  The Fellowship can be "hidden" until it is revealed by the hunt or declared by the Free People player (in order to get healed in a city or stronghold).  The Fellowship counter on the map is only an indication of its last known position.  When the Free People player uses a Character die to advance the counter on the Fellowship Progress Track, the Fellowship counter itself doesn't move.  The number on the track just means how far did the Fellowship travel. You count the number on the track from the last know position to an area that the Fellowship can reach.  This simple game mechanic really conveys the whole story atmosphere of hide and seek of the Fellowship effectively.

RATING: 8.4
The game mechanics portray very well Sauron's constant dilemma of "Search for the Ring now or fight the war?  Search for the Ring now or fight the war?", and the Good Guys' experience of "The bad guys outnumber us, but I have to accept this grinding series of defeats, because if I just give up, Sauron will be able to give his full attention to catching Frodo." .  

The plastic figures are attractively detailed, and there are almost two dozen different kinds, plus about a dozen more individual-character figures.  The map-board is pretty, especially to fans of the book.  

There is a simpler introductory game, and a full-rule-set version, marked chiefly by the addition of a lot of event cards (to let the players do particular things that happened in the novel), and more detailed (and more satisfying) hunt-for-the-ringbearer rules.  

I would say the full game rules will satisfy a deep [I]Lord of the Rings[/I]-geek—and only such a person would have the patience to learn them!  ;)

RATING: 6
[Played 25 times]
Wargame for 2. 
"Action dice" are a nice mix between limitations and freedoms (because the same symbol may be used in different ways and several actions may be accomplished with more than a symbol).
Asymmetrical (different cards, objectives, some rules, etc.) but balanced.
Luck is present (a lot), but can be partially managed. The most disappointing part is how much impact the (incontrollable) last turns (the "Mordor track") has on the final outcome.
Good replay value.
Nice flavor.
Poorly organized rulebook.
Excellent bits.

RATING: 9
Slugged through the complete rules the other night and played the full game.  People are right, play the full game and forget about the quickstart basic game.  Great game, very enjoyable.  Long but worth it.  FP cast the ring into Mount Doom.  I underestimated the hunt dice.  Don't forget to add dice to the hunt pool quite often!  Though, I did completely crush Gondor militarily.  The game did hold very close to the storyline which I liked.  I thought the Nazgul should have more powers than the simple leader powers that all leaders had.  Just my 2 cents.

3-12-08 - Upped it to 9 from 8.5 after several plays.  Excellent game system here!

RATING: 6
This game has an amazing theme and some good mechanics. However, the game is burdened by excessive rules: prepare for  a 40+ pages rule book, cards that can only be played under very specific circumstances (if faction x attacks you at location b after y, you can play this card), and printing player aids. If none of this deters you at all, you'll probably love this game. If you want a game that you can more or less grasp after your first play, this is not for you. I don't think this game has more depth than other heavier games like Imperial 2030 or Carson City, but it's a less elegant in it's design.

RATING: 9
Rating after five plays.  I like the game.  The theme is excellent, the tense decisions make it fun to play.  Sort of feels like a Twilight Struggle/Queen's Gambit combination, but much longer and with more rules.  Since those are two of my highest rated games, that is praise.  Has to played on the weekend for me, not a big deal.  The map is too small to handle the figures, the card type is very challenging even with my reading glasses (gramps!).  The rating may very well go up as I like games with some random elements and very tough decisions.  Oh, and since I don't paint, the figures are probably my biggest negative - a shame they weren't in different colors.  If someone gets a spare copy of the collector's edition they want to give to grandpa, I'd be mighty grateful ;-P

Moved the rating up to a 9 from an 8.  I doubt it will go higher due to various production issues and the occassional crazy swings due to the light combat system.  Is it as good as PR and Twilight Struggle?  I would say, almost, but not quite.

Now up to 10 plays.  I want to rate it a 10, but the components still nag at me some.  The cards are painfully hard to read - and I have up to date reading glasses.

First play with expansion was great!

RATING: 8
Complex, fiddly and slightly unbalanced but very good nevertheless! A great game that manages the rare feat of being both highly thematic and tactically rich with many options to explore in search of the win. Although very definitely not a card-driven wargame it has a similar feel thanks to the way your choices are limited by the action dice results, much like the way they're limited in a CDG according to your hand of cards. Multiplayer game is also interesting, although I do wish someone would come up with a non-team variant.

RATING: 9
After one play, I feel that this game hits so many right buttons. There are lots of meaningful decisions and options each turn, the components are excellent, the theme is as well integrated into the game as I've ever seen, there's practically no down time, there's a true difference between strategies and playing styles of the two sides.
The map, while great looking, does have problems, making it difficult to see settlements and fortifications, especially when armies are covering them. And distinguishing different units is difficult at first - I painted their bases different colors to solve that problem. 
But these, relatively minor, problems aside, this is truely an excellent game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
There was this girl in college...let's call her Darcy, for no particular reason. The first time I saw this game was like the first time I saw Darcy: I immediately wanted to love it. The first time I played this felt like the first time I met Darcy: I immediately did love it. Unlike Darcy, however, this game loved me right back in unspeakable ways and failed spectacularly to turn me into a bereft and bitter creature resigned to the futility of existence and the perpetual agony of squatting on the pointy end of a soul-sucking vortex of despair.

This game is [i]almost[/i] as good as I hoped Darcy would be; one cannot simply abandon reason, after all. But for the longest time, I assumed my deep and abiding wannabe-love would go unrequited just as it had with her.

At BGG.CON 2012, a kindly soul reached down into the sucking void and lifted me out by teaching me this and playing it--twice! That's twice in four days, which for me is pretty darn good, whether it's gaming or loving.

It's exactly like love should be: beautiful, deep, true, entrancing, captivating and challenging. And when you see it, there's probably no chance that you will be so nervous about asking it for some together time that you flee in shame and cower in your room convinced that you will die alone and unloved and hated by small animals, that last one for no particular reason except that it elevates your self-pity and self-loathing to new heights for entertainment's sake.

Reject the sweet lies spread by the shallow and the jealous: that it is unapproachable and vain and tedious. No, it goes deep and stays deep, and if you can't appreciate that, then you don't deserve her...err, it. Love it, and it will love you back.

I am--at long last--sated.

RATING: 8
I wasn't expecting much after reading the rules, but I was pleasantly surprised. The game neatly ties in many subsystems into a coherent whole that forces though, anguishing decisions on each turn. I wish there were more wargames like that out there. The simulation aspect of the game will also appeal LOTR fans; the rules allow you to relive the events of the book, or to explore what-if scenarios. Very nifty.

That being said, I have two concerns with the game. The first one is that it feels more random than it should (there are lots of results that only trigger on a roll of 6 on a D6, so a few die going one way or another can have an outsized result on the game). My other concern is that it seems that it seems almost impossible for the Shadows player to get 10 points in 12 turns??

Still, this is a very fun, tense game and I am looking forward to repeated playings.

RATING: 10
Great theme and fun to boot.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is the game that changed my gaming paradigm.  I have always had a bit of a problem with very chaotic/random games.  I rarely play/purchase overly random games, and many times I modify the rules to games I have purchased to reduce the randomness.  I don't have a lot of free time, so when I sit down to play a game I like to play against my opponent, not against his dice rolls.  That is, until I played WoTR.

This game is simply fun.  Don't misunderstand, there is a lot of thinking required and a multitude of decisions to be made, but they are largely limited to the 'action dice' rolls you have in a turn, and the cards you possess at the time.  Its hard to determine a long term strategy because you may not receive the dice rolls required to allow you to pursue it.  However, the short term tactics (essentially on a turn-by-turn basis) is very engaging.  The multitude of options due to the optimization needed to best utilize your action dice coupled with event cards; the large playing area and variety of pieces and objective areas; the multiple paths to victory; and the incredible theme - man this game is dripping with theme; come together to create a very fun game.  It is not the brain-burner that some of my wargames are, but for a lighter, decision-filled, super-replayable, well balanced, evening-playable, war-themed game, and especially for a LoTR fan, this game can't be beat.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Véletlenül vettem észre egy aukciós portálon, szemtelenül olcsón. Mindenképpen szerettem volna egy táblás wargame-et, érdekelt, hogy tetszene-e, hát gyorsan lecsaptam rá.
Több mint 200 darab szép kidolgozású - főleg a főszereplők! - műanyag figura, hatalmas játéktábla (a második kiadás táblája kicsit átláthatóbb, de ezt is meg lehet szokni hamar).
A játékszabály tanulmányozása - és az eredeti formátumba szerkesztése! :) -  legalább egy hétig tartott, nem nevezhető egyszerű játéknak a sok kis apróság miatt, de ebbe is hamar bele lehet jönni. De persze lehet, hogy csak kevés hasonló játékkal játszottam...
A játék azonban lenyűgözött! Valami hihetetlen profizmussal adja vissza a LoTR könyv élményét, nem véletlen az előkelő helye a Thematic rank-en is! Alapfelállásban a gonosz oldal annyival erősebb, hogy a wargame besorolás majdhogynem csak erre az oldalra érvényes. A szövetséges játékosnak nem sok esélye van így győzni, akárcsak az történetben kénytelen védeni magát és a harccal 'elterelni' a figyelmet Frodoékról, hogy a gyűrű megsemmisítésével megnyerhesse a játékot, ami szintén nem könnyű, érezni, hogy a nagy, vörösen izzó szem végig ott van rajtuk (gyűrűhajsza)! A karakterek megelevenednek, céljaik, sorsuk a könyv lapjairól egy az egyben került a játékba, de az eseménykártyák is ott és úgy használhatók fel, hogy a könyvhöz minél hűbb legyen a játék.
Számomra nagyon érdekes játékélményt nyújtott ez a játék - bár hossza és bonyolultsága miatt nem sűrűn kerül elő majd sajnos.
A játék után fiammal (gonosz oldal játékosa, természetesen) megnéztük a filmeket is. A játék alapján sok-sok részlet ismerős volt neki (bár ehhez a Knizia féle LoTR is hozzátett), de számomra is öröm volt felfedezni a sok hasonlóságot!

RATING: 10
Acquired at a UK Games convention in October 2004.   

Great fun it is too - loads of variables to conjure with, especially for the free peoples player.  

The rules aren't anything like as daunting as they first look!

What's impressive is how well it simulates the dynamics going on in Tolkien's masterworks. As the free people's player you stand a negligible chance of winning a military victory but you can pose just enough of a military threat to "distract" the eye player's attention from the fellowship providing you think laterally, keep the fellowship moving and are prepared to take a few risks.

Sure, it's imbalanced in favour of the shadow player but I don't have a problem with that - makes it feel true to the books.

I'm gonna have to paint those figures some time...

After 18 plays I upgraded my rating to a 10.  It gets better every time I play it and I find it difficult to imagine not enjoying this wonderful, wondrful game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm pretty disappointed by War of the Ring. First of all, let me state it is fun to play, even if it is long (for Euro standard). It has theme, it has character and it definitely has a "I see the story developing in front of me" factor that is quite unique. But, as a wargame, it's hardly great... let me explain why:
1) There's no maneuver. Having troops that slowly wander (1 space at a time) in such a large map simply makes maneuver impossible. No flanking, no desperate double marches to the rescue, nothing. Just one space moves, one space attack. The evil player mass up big armies, then overruns the free people, and this stops when a siege starts. Makes WWI looks dynamic. There are some moves that can actually be done as distraction, but I'd say 90% of the moves are just spent in massing up, moving up to the next objective and smashing. Finesse? Not available.
2) This leaves a system that seems to be based much on the administration of your resources instead of using them in clever way. This is in part true... but then, the dice comes. You roll the dice to decide your action, and this, epecially as a free people player conditions your action from a little to very much, depending on the dice rolled and the time of the game. I had a fast start with some lucky military victories which gained me time to move the fellowship more... but when I finished gaining time, I've got no character dice for three turns in a row. So the fellowship stayed still while the opponent got back in track from the early victory I had. Unability to seize themoment because I'm not that good at rolling the action dice? And you call this strategy? So the game hints that you should be good at using your resource, and then does half job on your behalf with those dice. The game then plays much more like an action/reaction system, where you on't do long term planning (only a very broad plan), and then follow your and your opponent dice wherever they take you.
3) If dice for actions are not enough in providing randomness for the game, the game throws at you buckets of dice for combat, then to hunt the fellowship, and to this the corruption tiles add another layer of luck. I know my game ended after four hours of player because I've drawed the wrong hunt tile while I was on the final stretch of the fellowship run, and then my opponent managed to roll a killing 5 on the dreaded die result. Disappointing, to say the least, as one roll of the die decided a 4 hours game. 
4) Finally the game has some complicated rules (fellowship rules spring to mind), by Euro standard, but then it has boring rules for armies (no characterization, all armies are identical, no matter if they had a lot of sculpts avilable), characters (this is almost ridiculous, the good characters are almost interchangeable with leaders 99% of the time, the evil chars are a little bit better however as far as characterization goes), cards (lots of cards with the same effects, a lots of cards that apply only on special situations, making them  interesting only if you have this or that character in a particular location when you draw it...), terrain...
In the end, I could end up playing this another time. Or two. But just for the fun factor involved... as far as wargames go, this is probably one of the most brainless dicefest I've tried.

RATING: 10
Near Perfect game. Amazing theme that feels natural with the mechanics. Elegant and clean game-play with lots of strategy, tension, fun, and even room for roleplay give the great interlock between theme and mechanics. Does a wonderful job of simulating everything that is Lord of the Rings. This game should have been called "Lord of the Rings" and Lord of the Rings the board game should have been called "Journey to Mount Doom" or something because this has all things war of the ring wonderfully simulated in a single game whereas LOTR the game focuses just on the journey of the hobbits. If you buy just one LOTR game or just one board game, make it this one. It's great even if you don't like LOTR. Everything from design to art to components to feel to originality in mechanics to the sense of urgency all the way to the climax--all of it is beautifully crafted.

We now have the second edition upgrade kit in addition to the Lords of Middle Earth expansion which largely overlaps with the expansion of the first game with a few ommissions and improvements. We've had the very difficult task of including the second edition upgrades with the expansion as well as elements from the first edition expansion that are not redundant with the new expansion (such as the ents or siege weapons). It was clunky at first but now I feel it makes the game that much richer though some of those first edition expansion components/mechanics such as ents, corsairs, and siege weapons are not used as much since they're less useful (probably why they were left out Lords of Middle Earth) still, thematically, it's nice that they're now available to us in case the situation calls for them.

I currently have second edition upgrade kit, lords of middle earth and soon warriors of middle earth.

RATING: 4
An intense and admirably detailed game that I'll probably never play again.  My third complete play convinced me that WotR, despite its numerous virtues, is just not worth the time investment it requires.  Why not?  Several reasons.  One, there are way too many rules.  Minor, easily forgettable, buried-in-the-manual rules.  Two, there are way too many exceptions to those rules.  I think the manual uses the word "except" more than any other in board game history.  Three, most of the cards have very nit-picky conditions for legal play, and you will find yourself unable to use anything in your hand with appalling frequency.  Four, the action dice, while not devoid of flexibility, are a poor method of reducing AP-related downtime.  No swords for the Free Peoples player(s) means no movement for Frodo.  No movement for Frodo means a 90% likelihood that the Shadow forces will win the game.  On the plus side, they might do it fast enough so that the good guys don't suffer much.  But if they don't, the game can be a long and grinding nightmare that you endure, rather than play.  A reluctant thumbs down for War of the Ring.

RATING: 9.5
Gorgeous game. To my mind this is universally one of the best adaptations of Tolkien's master-piece. Players that are fans of the Book will be given one of the most involving experiences they can ever hope to get. No spectator of the LotR film will ever be able to so accurately feel the intense clash of the free peoples/fellowship of the Ring against the Shadow Warriors. Simply fantastic!

An immense fantasy themed war strategy game. Set up time is above average. Playing time can be up to 2h30mins (do yourself a favour and don't ever try to play this with 4 players! this is a 2-player game!).

- Extensive rules that are not very well structured and might originate some confusion. Due to the different gaming styles fused into one game, reading these rules requires close attention and are best studied with the game set up on your table so you can better understand how it all works. Once you get it though, the game is fairly easy to play. Nevertheless, this is no game for newcomers!

[b]Strong theme[/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.2
Often 2-player games are very short, light, or quick playing.  None of these are bad things (in fact, most are considered quite desireable!) but sometimes that's just not what I'm looking for.  I'm looking for a meat and potatoes type of game, a game with lots of stuff going on where the fate of the universe/world/country is based on my decisions.  This role would normally be filled by a game such as Twilight Imperium (or perhaps even Axis and Allies), but until I played War of the Ring I had yet to be able to scratch that itch with just one other player. After one play my impression is that this game completely fills my need for a deep 2-player strategy game, and it is a lot of fun as well. 

I also feel this game does an amazing job of recreating the struggle for Middle Earth as read in the books (or seen in the movies).  While playing the game I feel like I am facing many of the same dilemnas that the fellowship (or sauron!) faced in the books, and yet always with a different twist to make the choice difficult.  Although I am a fan of the books and movies (movies moreso than the books, I'm not afraid to admit), I am certainly not a fanboy.  I just very much enjoyed the many 'what if?' scenarios that even one play of the game brought to the table.

I particularly like the 'action die' system.  Instead of each person having a long drawn out turn (ala Axis and Allies) each player is continually making decisions and actively participating.  Sure, not getting the actions that you need can be frustrating to a control freak, but I believe it just makes you have to adapt to the situation at hand.

Anyway, if you like games like TI and A&A and only ever seem to have one friend around willing to spend 4 hours of their life playing a board game with you, War of the Ring should fit the bill!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

The bad guy won in the end, but we actually did not have a clue what we were doing in the beginning so it really does not count.

I dearly would like to try it again soon.

Nine Year Old Son Catching the Nuances Upon His First Game: Priceless +0.50 Subjective 

I find very little not to like about this game.
Theme is utterly perfect-- everything seems to concur perfectly with the world of Tolkien.  The cards give flavor and speak to various actual events.  The characters affect the world in a meaningful and intelligent way.  This is Tolkien's war, as it should be.  

People complain about the graphics and card font.  Buy glasses.

People complain that the dice/cards often push them away from developing overarching strategies or that the system is too random.
This game is about balancing what you CAN ACTUALLY DO with what you NEED TO ACCOMPLISH.  One must maximize the use of the resources that one has available, each turn.

The mechanic supports the theme elegantly, by creating an atmosphere of attrition and defensive tactics (Free Peoples) versus territorial gain and unchecked military buildup (Shadow Armies)

My only complaints would be the game length and that it is decidedly a two player game only (from my view) unless you find playing with partnerships appealing.  This game is in every other way flawless. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
One play of this highly rated (Euro?) wargame, which really tests your dedication to the hobby. I have high respect that this game offers a strong visual and detailed narrative via some interesting and novel mechanics(simulating the book better than Knizia's designs), but sadly for me it also provides text overload, fiddly rules and play, and the real test of entertainment tolerance: the length of playing time; my interest waned after 2 hours of labourious reading & rule checks. This game was a reality check for me that I will never have the dedication nor the additude of a true gamer, I'm quite content with simpler fare; despite the strange narrative of Knizia's Middle Earth designs, especially The Confrontation, I admire their streamline functionality and short term pleasure they provide, but more importantly these are designs I have more chance of playing with loved ones. My rating reflects my gradually growing casual player attitude.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is THE Tolkein game. It defines the genre.  Of all the games it brings the true flavor of the War and the struggle to destroy the ring. Unlike other games (MECCG, LoTR Board game) this has it all in one easily accessible package. The only complaint I've seen that's truly justified is the small text on the cards, but you get used to it some how.  The map is beautiful, the pieces are awesome (you don't HAVE to paint them), the rulebook is attractive and well written (perhaps with some minor organizational problems), and the game play simply works.  I have no problems with the length of the game (4 hrs for the first game, much less after that), considering other games, like LoTR Risk, take just as long.  I would strongly suggest forgoing the "introductory game", it is little but an unfair game of risk, weighted strongly in favor of the sauron player.  Dive right into the richness of the standard game, break a few rules as you learn, but you will enjoy yourself so much more.  You can't beat this game for full imersion into Tolkein's world.  Update: After 10 plays, the game consistently takes only 2hrs, and I still love it and want to play more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I can't help but be amazed by this game.

Some have compared it to Risk-LOTR but I think the only thing it has to do with Risk is battle resolutions and then only because of dices you have to throw. There's much more to it than just dices : battle action cards that can change much, elite regiments and officers. There's also a different kind of battle (one that can go on for several 'dice-use') when there's a siege and that reminds me of Hannibal's system.

I don't think it's correct to assume Free-people's player has far less to do because he has less action dices : the Dark Lord must spend several dices tracking down the Fellowship if he wants to avoid the destruction of the One Ring. So, all in all, they almost have an equal number of actions to play on each turn. However there are a few Mordor action cards  that are truely too powerful.

Just a question : why are some people rating it at 1 when the, obviously, haven't played the game ?

As the Free People player, move your companions group as far as you can or try to get two shadow stronghold. Fantastic strategy game with a lot of variants and possibilities, perfect for group gaming. Mix of cards, figures, strategy and one of the best written story ever. Superb components quality. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Takes a lo-o-o-ong time to learn, and our first few games ran at least six hours each. Nowadays we can usually finish in the advertised 3 hours or less. But even at six hours, the game is riveting for the entire time!

While this [i]is[/i] a wargame, it is not [i]just[/i] a wargame. It is amazingly rich in theme, evoking the novels on every play without ever forcing the action to follow the literary plot. You must not only maneuver your armies, but also make the right choices for around ten different individual characters. As the Free Peoples player you must keep Sauron's armies at bay and try to sneak the Ring into Mordor without allowing the Ringbearer to be completely corrupted by the Ring. As the Shadow Forces, you must divide your attention and your resources between your attempts to crush the Armies of the West and your urgent search for the Ring!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Solid game - not brilliant but not a disaster.  Captures the feel of the novel well, but also feels too scripted.  Pacing is a bit off: as much time is spent merely marching reinforcements up to the front; a more clever design could have abstracted this and cut down the playing time. The set-up is a bit too fiddly, especially with very similar looking miniatures.  Finally the cards don't quite work: they don't add much tension and in some ways are not random enough!  Some events are good and will always appear (e.g. knockout Saruman) where others will never appear.  More constraint would ensure the game played differently every time.  As it stands, there is little tension: if you have a good card you just hold it 'til you play get a chance to play it.  But, it works, integrates characters and armies, and covers the full scope of Middle Earth.  It also usually comes down to a tense climax, as each player is running a different race, and speed in your race tends to also mean speed for the other player in his race: this does however exacerbate the role of chance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.25
Based on one play, may change later.  There are lots of German games and lots of wargames I would rather play.  I have a very hard time understanding all the ten ratings.  This is a long, slow game with too much randomness for that sort of commitment of time.  Disliked how my turn's strategy was chosen randomly and then I had to adapt my tactics to match this random die roll.   Furthermore, the graphic design is terrible!  It LOOKS good, but borders are hard to distinguish, imporant locations are tiny and buried under counters, many important regions are too small to hold the armies that should be put into them (and stacking counters under minitures like A&A does not work well because the counters are _slick_ & don't stack well), card sizes and fonts are way too small, etc., etc.  The Fellowship Player has far fewer choices than the Dark Power player, which is not fun if you are the Fellowship. 
(Overall, I suspect if I play it more my rating will drop...)
This game was obviously based on SPI's War of the Ring game.  What the designer did was take that counter fest and try to make it easier for newcomers. Shame he could not have used the font that SPI used on its event cards.
Trying to figure out the good points / attractive qualities of this game:  The theme is what makes this game fun, if not for that I would expect at least a 2 point drop in its ratings.  I suspect that the theme has enouraged a large group of people ready for bigger / longer games to try one and that is a good thing.  
UPDATE: Thought of another thing that some people may like.  Many games with lots of choices suffer from 'analysis paralysis' (not a problem in my group thankfully).  With this game your stragetgic choices are made RANDOMLY and you have to adapt.  No paralysis for newbies who would freeze up with a big game.  That suggests to me that it may be a transition game from light games to heavier strategic games.  Still have hard time understanding all the ten ratings tho.

RATING: 8.5
This is certainly one of best thematic games. The miniatures, the map and the artwork on the cards are gorgeous. After one (and a half) plays it seems as there are a lot of strategies to try out. The Mordor player is certainly creating the game (is he giving the fellowship opportunities to advance; does he attack Gondor, Rohan or the Elves?), while the fellowship player rather reacts to this. 

In general I am not a big fan of reading a bunch of cards in each turn. But here I can overlook this easily, since the cards really offer a lot of different possibilities and increase the thematic flair of the game. The only down side I see so far for this game is the very long setup time and the rules. The rule book is well written, but there are just a lot of rules to remember (especially regarding politics) and you will have to take a look in the rule book several times during first play.

RATING: 4.5
tl;dr: Not a bad game, but probably not a game for me.

I've read the rulebook & the Universal head instructions multiple times.  I've set this game up and run through 3 - 4 turns a number of times mostly by myself, but once with my wife.  I just can't muster the gumption to care.

I love the idea of a board game encompassing all aspects of the War of the Ring.  But this game just kills the intrigue for me.  I think it has to do with all the special-case rules.  Rules for moving the fellowship, splitting off from the fellowship, fighting in the field, fighting in a siege, each card in the game is its own set of rules.  The expansion makes the problem worse.

Like I said, I love the idea of all these, but when it comes to actual play, I get bogged down and don't want to actually do anything because I don't want to have to look it up.

Even when I do finally grasp it all, the strategy for what to do next isn't obvious.  Even if I pursue trying to achieve the conditions for a particular event card - pulling it off seems to be only a marginal improvement in my situation.  That's probably by design.

By that point, I've invested HOURS of effort in reading, setting up, and actually playing, and I still don't care.  I don't have that kind of time these days to invest in something I get little satisfaction from.

My reaction is a little weird since I really like TI3 and both these games strike me as having a similar "weightiness", so maybe it's just the theme?

I'd be willing to play it with an enthusiastic partner, but I doubt I'll ever suggest it.

RATING: 9
Excellent 2-player wargame that really captures the flavour of one of my favourite books (and now also movies). Both players are constantly on the lookout for opportunities for themselves, and potential pitfalls for their opponent (often determined by the strategy cards), and all the while trying to play according to a grand strategy. More balanced than the uneven distribution of units would seem to imply.

And, yes, it does play much better and easier now that I painted the bases in the nations' colours. I also added the different city size bits from Sid Meier's Civilization to serve as towns and cities, and the fortifications from History of the World (Hasbro) for the strongholds (they nicely contain a city from Civ). This makes the board a lot clearer. In case the board should become too crowded in places, I use the plastic chips from Axis & Allies as replacements. Works better than those cardboard chits in the game.

RATING: 6
Revised rating:  Extremely onerous to set up and play - moreso even than any of the big hex-and-counter games I've played. Payoff just isn't worth the work, especially once you get that deja-vu feeling from the scripted play. 

-----------------------------------

Various mechanics hang together well. Dunking the ring sub-game is genius, as is interactive action die system.  Great interplay between characters, events and battles. 

Hurt by the limited options available to Fellowship player. Play becomes predictible with experienced players as much of map is best ignored. 

Still, will produce tremendous narrative game experiences before strategic limitations become apparent.

Overall a very good game, a must for LoTR fans and a valuable one for the rest.
