RATING: 7
In my opinion, the best of the strategic game adaptations of Tolkien. Plays well with 2,3 0r 4. 

Plastic pieces are fiddly and too big but, the characters almost demand this.

The game can be decided by outright military victory, destruction of the ring or corruption of the ring bearer. Players have limited resources in their decks and must choose on which arena to focus.

Some of the rules are tough to resolve at first but, ultimately work well.

15-06-2014. Rated WotR 1st edition a 8.9, version 2nd editions 9. Difference can be explained by the better and larger cards, bigger regions on the board ánd the better marking of VPs each stronghold is worth on the board.

04-03-2014. Finally played War of the Ring. Admittedly, I now rate my own copy of the first edition, but regardless the game is largely similar except some minor tweaks.

I adore this game. There is so much to do, and so many interesting things to do. Because we were both new to the game, I won on 4 military VPs, by attacking both Isengard and Moria. Epic! I survived by bringing my heroes there for extra battle dice.

Currently, I'm loving this game. I must say, that I am not yet sure on how/if my rating changes. But currently I love it and would really want to play again.

RATING: 5
Only 1 play each of the quick-start and standard rules. But I don't see this hitting the table again.  The theme is pretty well integrated but mechanically it just feels like two games, an asymetric, more complex version of Risk on one side and a very race/push your luck game to destroy the ring without getting corrupted on the other side. I know that's what the last 2 books are like, basically 2 separate story lines, but I would have liked to see the Risk part perhaps simplified and the hunt aspect more interesting, with the Sauron player getting to use the Nazgul more actively to hunt and the Free Peoples player doing something more interesting than "I use a Character die to move the Fellowship forward one", and again, and again.

RATING: 8
[b]October 2013 Gifted to a friend who is much more likely to play it[/b] - Will probably look into purchasing the 2nd edition in the future.

Must...play...more...If I had the time. It's complex, it takes a lot of time, and it takes a lot of space.

But, the complexity gives it so much flavor and really helps to model the politics and the struggles depicted in the book. The breadth of the game feels truly epic as you struggle to fight a successfull delaying action or impose your will with extreme brutality on the rest of the world. The space the game occupies as well as the detailed miniatures as well as the use of the cards and map really add to the epic feel.

As a Tolkien fan, I love it. As a guy who loves ameritrash type wargames, I think it successfully depicts an assymmetric war that both sides have a chance to win. There are heroic last stands, brutal combats, gallant heroes, unforgiving decisions, tide turning events, and a hellish gamble of a journey against all odds.

The liklieness of the Shadow sacking multiple free peoples cities is very likely, even though they didn't take any in the books. Thematically, that's one of the big differences with the books, but I believe it's okay in this game as it gives a lot of tension to the game.

If you've got time and an interest in the theme or an interest in this type of war game, give it a try. I've thoroughly enjoyed my couple plays. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
dropping and dropping. Quickly down to a point where I (almost) always refuse to play. Gorgeous and superbly themed, yes. But there's so much not to like: Playing and set-up time is beyond excuse (less would've been MUCH more). The rules are UNNECESSARILY complex, nothing is solved elegantly. The German translation is errenous. The lay-out of the board is crappy to say the least, regions and settlements impossible to make out. But worst of all, I have a huge problem with two fundamental concepts of the game: The action dice are quite frankly a bad idea. And the strategy cards are a pain in the ass. Either nearly useless or game-clinching, it's important when they pop up RANDOMLY.  And then game-breaking events are decided by dice.  All games I've played so far have been decided by a serious of fortunate dice rolls. I like that in a 45-minute romp, but in a 4 hour game it's quite frankly intolarable to me.  Sad to see that so much potential has been wasted.

RATING: 8.5
I really really love this game.  However, were it not for the love of the Tolkien universe, I may not like it so much.  It has real ambition, but some real design issues as well.  These are primarily component quality/aesthetic, as I see it.  It is very hard to discern/locate regions on the board.  I'm not so sure the "subtle earth tones" theme really works well for logistically for a board depicting a fictitious landmass with tons of borders--likewise, many pieces look identical upon initial glance, and are unpainted plastic that has been injection molded using material of the same color.  These are of so-so quality (or were with the intial release of the game, anyway)--about half of mine were warped or bent upon opening the box (these issues were apparently common to many copies upon release).  A game this expensive should have added a few bucks more and at least had pieces of unique color!!  Painting individual pieces would be perfect, but that would be monstrous for production and tedious for gamers who don't put that type of effort into their hundreds of games (like me!). Ok, enough grousing...it's very rich and detailed0--I doubt very much that many twelve year olds would find this game approachable--but there are no doubt some who would (particularly fans of combat/fantasy games, Tolkien fans, or both).  It's hard enough for me and my thirty/forty-something year-old friends with masters and PhDs to wrap our intellect around.  Still, I love the game, as I said.  Richly detailed, satisfyingly intricate, and not too long--one of those games where playing honestly gets a lot faster once players are more familiar with its turn mechanic (see my Turn Summary document for help with that!).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My friend refers to WotR as a "historical simulation," and that's very much what it is. It's a tremendously successful effort at representing "what if" scenarios for the War of the Ring. The basic concept of the military and Ring victory conditions is quite clever and really motivates the game to follow courses that, while individually different, all seem appropriate for the story. 

The game does take a while to play, and I know that my appreciation of the setting, stories and movies makes that less annoying than it might be for a theme I didn't care about. It also has the "problem" that game time can vary rather a bit depending on how the game goes, but any wargamer ought to be used to this. Hand in hand with the potential length is the fact that a lot comes down to the endgame, which might frustrate you if the game has been long and you're not in it for the process of play. I've read reviews and game sessions where a game of WotR comes off as an exercise rather than an experience. For me, it's firmly in the experience category, as I think my session reports will attest. I'm not looking to figure out the optimal win strategy and then stick to it. I want to know what happens if the Easterlings strike at the Dwarves, or if the Fellowship sticks together to the bitter end to defender the Ringbearer. With that in mind, I won't become stuck in a rut and focus on the deficiencies of WotR rather than its strengths.

Overall, I really enjoy WotR and wish I had more time to play it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
There are lots of things in this game I like, but what I love is that every single game seems to be close because of the victory condition setup. In most of my games the Fellowship is poised to destroy the ring in Mount Doom, while Shadow is a few corruption points away from a ring victory himself, while also being ready to take his last settlement for a military victory. *Every* game I've played swells to a climactic dramatic finish. Once you know what you're doing, this game plays in 2-3 hours (not 4-5 marathons than people are complaining about). ***UPDATE: There are a few things which I don't like about this game, but what I hate is the compounded luck that can completely derail any attempt at strategic play. Dice rolls for your turn actions, random card draws (with many of different effectiveness than the others), dice rolls in combat, dice rolls in the hunt, random tile draws for hunt damage, and once you get to Mordor just total luckfest as to whether you draw a good or bad tile. The amount of luck in a "strategic" game this long is really ruining it for me. This started as an 8, rose to a 9, and is now falling towards a 7.

Its a race game, where the Fellowship is trying to dunk the ring while the Shadow is trying to conquer the free peoples. Strategy comes in as you try to speed yourself up and slow down the enemy. Fellowship can slow down the shadow by guessing where he is going to attack and mustering troops there, and by splitting off companions to these places to help fight. The Shadow can slow down the Free Peoples by hounding the fellowship with Nazgul and armies (for rerolls) and praying to god that he rolls good on his hunt rolls. But there is no depth to these strategies. "He is attacking Gondor...I guess my strategy should be...to muster in Gondor?" DUH! You don't decide your strategy either, the game does, by the cards you are given and the dice you roll. I actually go into the game thinking "If I draw this card I'll do this, if I get that card I'll do that". Right now, I'm still happy to play the game if someone asks. Once I start saying "um...lets play this instead", I'll drop this to a 7 (and despite all my moaning, I don't believe this game deserves less than that).

My summary of feelings on this game is very much what I feel about PlanetSide. The problem with both games is that they're unique, but just not very good. So if you want to play a game like them you've got no other alternatives. And thats the only thing that brings me back to each. ***ANOTHER UPDATE: Ok, I lied, I DO believe it deserves less than a 7. I will only ever play this game again if I can be the fellowship, and then I'll just go on autopilot, moving the fellowship as much as I can and mustering where its needed. The only thing I still like in the game are little dwarf figures. They are the funniest. ****FINAL UPDATE?: Replayed this game 3 times recently. Just does nothing for me anymore. Way too long for even setup let alone the game, I just get frustrated when I lose, and barely feel anything good if I win. The only thing that still attracts me to the game are the aesthetics. The figures are funny and the map is pretty.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Bought this to have a crack at painting the miniatures. Once finished, plunged in with the standard game, which went down surprisingly well with the missus. Beautifully produced game, clever and engaging mechanics. Knowledge of Middle-Earth geography and LOTR is very helpful, especially as map board is difficult to read once loaded with miniatures, but I didn't find the issues so bad as others. Took about 5 hours for our first game - had no idea how much time had passed. Exceeded expectations.

Shame about the lack of Gollum, Gandalf the White and Aragorn miniatures. Look forward to acquiring the expansion, hope the makers think up some other expansions or variations for the future. In many ways the core mechanics seem very similar to Blue Vs Gray - so a Civil War game or other historical conflict would be nice. This would surely be the mechanics and production values best suited to an epic Genghis Khan game!

I am honestly not obsessed with LOTR, but too many of the games are just so damned good.

Edit: upped rating to 10, which it is impossible to deny this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Not sure what I think of this game yet. I played one game as the Shadow Player, losing to a final hunt tile pluck that had a 40% chance of succeeding. I was a little saddened that after hours (and hours) of playing the game basically came down to a coin flip. Or maybe I'm just ticked that I had 9 victory points and was bearing down on the city of Dale with a military victory in my grasp... sigh! But anyhow, War of the Ring is seeping with theme; if you're a Tolkien fan, this game is an absolute must. The game has a number of interesting mechanisms, but despite them (or because of them) there is a tremendous amount of luck to contend with. Action dice rolls shape your possible course of actions, which is cool. The cards you draw and when you draw them can have a significant effect on your game, which is not all that cool, but I can see that knowing what ALL the cards are could help you plan ahead, and possibly mitigate the luck somewhat. I don't like that there are certain critical game moments with severe consequences that require a lucky roll to surpass. I felt fairly hopeless as Sauron to hinder the fellowship's journey, there's just so much luck involved. I discovered as time went on that there were some subtle, defensive things I could do, but it's maddening to watch to ring-bearers zip through your lines like you're invisible. The "fellowship hidden movement" system was a fascinating implementation that borrows from Schroedinger's Cat. You invest in move points, which increase your range of "how far the ring bearers are away from their last known position", and then at some point you declare which region they're actually in. It's bizarre, it's "collapse the probability wave." Frodo's Cat. Anyhow, I'm rambling. There's a lot to this game, and a hefty learning curve. It's worth another play to figure out how I really feel.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10

[url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/428901/a-timeless-epic-struggle]My review is here[/url].

Ignoring the theme, this is already a tough, excellent game, which presents agonizing decisions involving the dual-purpose cards and innovative mechanism of action dice. Add on the Lord of the Rings theme, and this game is an automatic 10, because the theme is integrated so seamlessly, so beautifully with the mechanisms. Look through your discard piles after you finish the game, and you can visualize an alternative history of Middle-Earth that is different from what you know, yet entirely plausible. I have seen the theme fester on the emotions of the players: both players start out joking about the theme and noting how cool it is as they do things that just "make sense". Then the chatter dies a slow and eerie death as the shit hits the fan. The FP player feels the tension first, the despair of a defensive war fought on too many fronts and with too little in reserve, and the frustration of the dice going horribly wrong in a siege (the FP player always gets REALLY moody midway during the game). This culminates in an ultimate crisis of faith when he realizes there's no way in hell he's going to win. Then he remembers Frodo and Sam with renewed urgency. All this sound familiar? This roller-coaster of emotions will happen to the FP player in the game, no matter how prepared you are for it. Soon later, the Shadow player feels a different sort of tension, an annoyance at that little Fellowship cockroach evading capture time and again and bringing your bomb closer, and impatience at those unexpected delays you keep incurring while trying to gain that decisive victory and break. That. Stronghold.

The genius of the game is the asymmetry of the two sides perfectly capturing the situation in the novels and movies. This is a war fought on two fronts - the military domination of Middle-Earth and the hunt for the Ring - with each side having the initiative in one of them, and an outside chance at a secondary victory if the opponent screws up. The Free Peoples start in disarray, reluctant to fight, while the Shadow is able to muster its full strength early, yet must step carefully to avoid provoking the Free Peoples to band together. The fascinating concept in the divergent goals is that of time. Both sides possess a ticking time bomb which they attach to the other at the start of the game, and the loser is the side whose time runs out first. The Shadow player tightens the temporal noose through military victories, while the FP player challenges the Shadow player to quickly level those surprisingly resilient strongholds through movement of the One Ring. Both sides have limited capability to extend their time limit a little bit for some breathing room: the FP through hapless defenses barely thrown together in the nick of time; and the Shadow through a focus on the Ring and a blighting on the probable path the Fellowship is taking. But do not be tempted to simply react and forget your goal. The winner is always he who made his opponent's bomb tick faster.

The struggle of time (or rather, this timeless struggle!) is delicate and snowballs from tiny innocuous actions (or lack thereof). Huge momentum swings happen during the game as the little things done or not done add up through subtle means (mostly through action dice effects). Momentum will swing back and forth 2-4 times in the game, and the key is to seize that pendulum and never let it go again.

In no other game is the flow of war so drastic, so devastating, and so riveting. "Game" doesn't do War of the Ring justice. This is an epic.

5+5

Really, I should dock this 0.5 because of some questionable decisions regarding the components, and because this version can suck it next to my COLLECTOR'S EDITION.

RATING: 9.75
WotR is my sentimental favorite as it was my introduction to true Geek-dom. The narrative and immersion is tops. I regard this game so highly that it's easier to ask, so, why not a perfect score? The 2nd Edition re-ignited my zeal for this game, but there are remaining negatives:
 
- The card-management aspect of the game is the weak spot for me; This is my biggest source of analysis-paralysis. It's simpler for the Shadow since that side has more dice resources; but the Free Peoples have to be much more discrete. The use/discard decisions are often difficult and bogs down the gameplay. 

This is exacerbated by the fact that the cards serve a dual purpose as battle modifiers. Also, effects of cards played on the table are often forgotten. This all serves to busy an already complicated game. Maybe my brain is too small to optimize the cards' use. This make Hulk angry. Hulk smash.
 
- The game is so epic and expansive that sessions can last several hours and lead to gaming fatigue. In a tight game, the machinations of climbing Mt. Doom could play out like the bottom half-inning a pivotal baseball game that's already stretched 13 innings. (You'd think you'd love that, but sometimes you don't. Sometimes you just want to call it a night or play a different game.)

RATING: 8
Ok, so I decided to give this game another chance and I realized as I was playing it that it was just so much more fun for me to play the forces of Darkness than the Fellowship.  After putting time into the game I have had the chance to see how enjoyable it really is.  Perhaps my opinion will change about the Fellowship eventually, but mostly it seems like you just take a beating and hope to hold out until you slam dunk the ring into the mountain.

Yes, the dice mechanic was done first in Minos and questionably better.  But there usually are a number of great options available with what you roll. The reason I can't give a higher rating for this is that a couple bad action rolls can be devastating.  The system does have the potential to clump in awkward ways (Mordor/Fellowship tile cards stuck at the bottom, no mustering in the begining, all muster at the end, no swords rolled etc.)  

I have a love/hate relationship with the cards.  I tend to not like a bunch of crap on a bunch of cards in games. On one hand the cards add excitement and color, but really slow the pace of the game down and often you are reliant on good draws.

Regardless, it is quite fun and beautifully evocative.

Mar 2005: Haven't played a full game - just got a demo (very poorly run!) at GenCon '04. While it has some interesting mechanics - the race to destroy the ring, the Shadow's search for the fellowship - it seems to be too much of a wargame for me. Sep 2011: 2er, full game, with someone who knows the rules.
MUCH better! The physical production is still a bit iffy -- pieces don't fit in regions, Nazgul are top heavy and need pennies glued to the bottoms to keep them standing, etc. -- but the game play is greatly improved playing this as a 2-player game. Gameplay is very evocative of the theme and story. And there are some very cool mechanisms here: 
- the dice-driven action selection narrow the scope of what you can do, which is good for a non-wargamer!, but _should_ average out over time
- the search for the Fellowship by reducing your dice pool ... very nice! In fact I like pretty much everything about how the Fellowship is handled: it must break over time, as people are forced to split off to do things; terrific semi-hidden movement system that is both simple and effective in hiding exact location without becoming cumbersome; how Sauron catches glimpses as they move, are found, then hide again.
- event cards and dice sort of remind me of card-driven (war)games like Twilight Struggle, which BTW is an awesome game!
- combat is tough! Clever to have Sauron's forces unlimited, but have the Free Peoples succumb to attrition through the game.
- pretty simple turn structure, really, for such an involved game
- the final push up Mount Doom to destroy the ring is appropriately tense!

I'm not sure how much I will like it in the long run, though: there are an awful lot of dice rolling here and I tend not to like those types of games. And the game is somewhat long, although that isn't necessarily a problem for me. But looking forward to trying it again!
7/10?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Absolutely dripping with theme. Personally I find the luck well balanced. There's just enough dice rolling to feel like you're balancing out bad luck without turning the game into a dicefest, and the cards, although varying in usefulness do not feel overpowered in the context of the game.

I am slightly wary about limited strategy options, but as I never bought the game intending to play it 50 times in as many days, we probably won't be hitting the problem until long after I've had very good value for money from it.

RATING: 7.5
Quite a fun, tense game that really evokes the feel of the LotR novels.  I do have two complaints that drop the rating, however:

1)  For a game that should take about 2 hours, you have to factor in about an hour for setup and teardown -- so in that respect, I'm not sure the payoff is really worth the amount of time spent NOT playing the game itself.

2)  Some of the pieces are VERY fiddly, in particular the Nazgul, which really don't want to stand up.  And while I get the point of the tokens for stacking multiple units, the board still gets rather cluttered rather fast, and moving large armies can be a pain in the ass.

Still, lots of fun here, though I don't think I'd pick WotR over something like Twilight Struggle if given my druthers.

RATING: 10
A superb treatment of the novel; better than I ever expected to see. The dynamics between the military game and the simultaneous quest game make an intriguing play experience. Only downside is that new players must invest some time absorbing the rules and play two or three times before understanding enough to play without feeling lost.

In my opinion this was a breakthrough design, one which demonstrated that in some cases an entirely new approach can be clearly superior to a top shelf hex and counter wargame design. Richard Berg and SPI put their all into their War of the Ring game, and at one time I owned the deluxe mounted map edition. It wasn't as good as this game; not even close.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow!!  WOW!!!!  What a truly EPIC game!!  This is my favourite board game of all time for 2 players (although when I don't have several hours to commit to a game, [thing=21763][/thing] is my favourite board game of all time for 2 players), particularly  when combined with the expansion elements of "Battles of the Third Age", and when played with BGG user Jimzik's "Starting from the Shire" variant (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/111865).  It is also about the most complex board game that I could ever tolerate playing, and it would certainly be too long and complex for me if I wasn't such a big Lord of the Rings fan.  The level of complexity really brings the books to life, but if you are not a fan of heavy games or are not a Lord of the Rings fan, you will probably want to avoid this one.  And it certainly is a beast to just get to the table - prior to our first game I spent dozens of hours spray-painting the minis, reading and re-reading and re-re-reading the rulebooks and FAQs in an attempt to wrap my head around the countless rules exceptions, searching BGG for player aids and rules summaries, and scavenging from other games for settlement markers and other game pieces.  The problem is, despite being an absolutely incredible game, the graphic designers have seemingly done everything in their power to confound the novice player - everything from monochromatic figures, to map regions that are too small to fit the figures, to barely discernible nation borders, to settlement symbols and region names that fade into the board background, to bizarre nation symbols on the political track and elsewhere, to minuscule card text that requires a magnifying glass, to a terribly organized rulebook, make this a very difficult and frustrating game from a visual standpoint.  I've gone to the extensive effort of correcting most of these problems with my game, and the work was definitely worthwhile (you can do the same - have a look here: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/38850).  If you have one willing opponent and several hours to set up and play through a game, it simply does not get better than this!  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 5/5  Total: 9/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
If there's a game that betters War of the Ring in terms of delivering a fantastic, nail-biting experience while expertly balancing narrative and strategic gameplay, I haven't tried it (Update: I've at least now played its equal and it's Twilight Struggle).  Many strategy games tend to make concessions when it comes to theme to ensure the mechanics are accessible to new players, or the thematic elements are only pin-on dressings with just a tenuous attachment to what the player is actually doing.  Not so, here.  This game drops the players right in the middle of a warring Middle Earth.  The players are first and foremost competing for victory, but every single action taken towards this goal compound to create an exciting and engaging alternate telling of the Lord of the Rings epic.  When playing, I keep switching between grinning like a kid because of the cool situations that are unfolding and scratching my head trying to decide what would be the best course of action.

There are some nitpicks about the production quality that can be made (small text on the event cards, awkward board for handling so many pieces, overly esoteric and subtle symbols to differentiate regions and nationalities) but, really, these don't detract from my enjoyment of the game one iota as these problems are greatly outweighed by what this gorgeous production does right.

(My one worry: I hope the first 95% of the game isn't essentially pointless and that the victor isn't always decided by luck of the draw once (if) the Fellowship enters Mordor.)

UPDATE: The game is surprisingly good with 4 players.  I wouldn't have expected that.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
War of the Ring has a lot going for it, but if I can name only 1 thing that stands out, it's this: You're IN the books/movies, living the story and seeing how it could have evolved instead.

Whereas the Free Peoples Player has to work with nations that don't want to go to war, limited resources and ultimately gambling a lot on the Fellowship, the Shadow Player has a big but slow army, and needs to divide his resources between the military, and the hunt for the ring. It's a brilliant recreation of the story, and it works.

Most of the exciting stuff that happened in the books can be reproduced in the game, but the game's also open enough to allow many different events to occur.

If there's a drawback, it's that the various systems themselves (the political track, the hunt...) are too complicated, and could have been simplified. As it stands, every time there's a chance this game will hit the table, I have to restudy many of its systems. And that's a big drawback unfortunately.

RATING: 8
Rating based on 1 play.

No real problem with rules, but did have FAQ.

Beautiful game and deep play.

If you love Lord of the Rings then you should at least TRY this even if you are not a wargamer or a heavy gamer.  It has loads of theme. 

I imagine this is immersive and loads of fun as you get more comfortable with it.  I look forward to getting there!

Update: 3 more plays and the rating moves to an 8.  The game is obviously well designed and brings it's subject matter to life about as well as one could hope.  There are a LOT of little rules that seem to be included primarily for sake of theme.  I wonder if these could be trimmed without effecting gameplay significantly.  I am waaaay to new to this game to make an informed judgement about that.

I can see the rating moving up to a 9 or back to a 7.5 depending on how fast future games play, but it will likely never be lower than 7.5.   



Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 1
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 1


Overall - 1 (The anti-Travel game. There is no way to even try to make this a reasonable travel size)

RATING: 9.5
Pretty damn good for the amount of replay I've gotten out of it.

"there is a card in here that lets an army with a leader move two spaces!" you should be fine.  Anyway, it is the most flavorful LOTR game I've played, and being the LOTR fan I am that is enough to move it to my top game list.

The main negative I can think of is that the free peoples may not be as fun to play for some.  You basically move the ring as much as you can and react to the Sauron player.  Not as much opportunity to plan cool strategies as shadow imo.

RATING: 8
A good 7 that has a special place because it renders the story with brilliance. Only for Middle Earth lovers as it depends on that.

*

The Risk-like conflict mechanics are saved by the brilliant fellowship travel mechanic, different endgame conditions, card mechanics that grant different strategies & tactics game to game, and very well conceived flavor that provides the chrome more a feeling of depth than gloss. You can tell the design was built outword from the story, and if you love Tolkien it's a treat.

I painted the characters in detail, and gave the leaders of each group a monocolor paint job. And I replaced the elite and regular troops with dyed wood discs & squares, matching their colors. This vastly improves the readability of the gamestate - it no longer looks like a crowded bus - and imho the aesthetics are improved.

RATING: 4
An overly long, detailed, and somewhat "risk-like" version of Lord of the Rings.  The ring-bearer is mostly disconnected from the battle stuff, the politics of rousing the west against the forces of Sauron is just a matter of time, and the combat system isn't that interesting.

It's an example of check-the-box design, where most events in the trilogy get an event card somewhere with flavor text.  That's not the same as recreating the essential tensions of the book: Can the west be roused in time?  Can Sauron be distracted from the ring-bearer's quest?

RATING: 7
I have this game in my collection since 2005. Mainly because of the daunting rulebook, the daunting FAQ and a messy gameplay test in 2006 I never properly played it. Until yesterday evening, that is. It took us six hours to play, but it was a great experience. Cannot believe how incredibly faithful to the book (and film) this game is - it is really dripping with theme and full of drama, allowing for some great showdowns at key locations on the board. The only minor complaint I have after playing a single game is that some of the cards seem a bit too powerful, but that might just be a matter of experience.
Still, the game will probably sit on my shelf for another year before it hits the table again. It takes too much time to play on a regular evening, and requires a second player who is just as dedicated to playing such a complex game as me. My advice for your first game: Definitely have both players read the rules and be sure to print one of the great summaries created by BGG users.
Now, did this game not have an expansion?...

RATING: 9
02/2011:9
I am not usually a big fan of war games, but War of the Ring is a magnificent design. This game captures the essence of LOTR and gets a big thumbs up. The game is extremely thematic and card play clear reflects progression of the story line. I especially enjoy the hunt tiles and how it affects movement of the fellowship. The fellowship part of the game was extremely well designed and well integrated into the game play. I am less pleased with how the battles are conducted. The battles look relatively simplistic and as the free peoples, your choices are rather limited. Which brings me to my biggest worry: the replayability of this game is limited, at least I suspect. The starting armies and strategies are similar and i could imagine, or time, a dominant strategy will emerge. Still, it is a very well designed game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Only 1 play so far. This one is very difficult to rate. 

For first, it takes way too long to setup and quite too long time to play. 

The graphics and miniatures are very cool, but make the game play bit difficult and annoying from time to time 
I was playing the Mordor and failed to notice that you can directly move from the north to Minas Morgul... which cost me the game actually. The map is a bit difficult to read and manage.

Also, I'm not 100% convinced that the action dice are optimal decision limiting tool for such a long game. You can get very much screwed by the dice. For example, in our play the fellowship did not throw a single fellowship move (nor the joker action) during his 3 first turns. That's pretty bad.

However, I like many of the mechanics. I like how the fellowship works and how you get more dice etc. Mechanics go very well hand in hand with the excellent theme. I liked the feel of the game, it really feels like playing a LotR game.

There is slightly too many minor rules for my taste. Even so they work well with the great theme, they tend to slow the game and it is very difficult to keep them all in your head, when playing.

Anyway, I still want to try it again. It had lots of upsides too.

RATING: 9
Each side has two paths to victory, and concentrating on one allows your enemy to get the upper hand in the other.  So it's self balancing without having the sides be identical.  Impressive design.  Flavorful, too.

Would be a ten but docked a point for poor human factors engineering: microscopic card text, inscrutable heraldic emblems, minis too similar and too big for their regions, map could've been better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Arguably the most cohesive and faithful treatment of a theme out there.  The Shadow player is trying desperately to find the ringbearer, but also  managing an all-out offensive.  Meanwhile, the Free Peoples player has to wisely manage his risk in moving the fellowship, while trying to apply his cards to their greatest effect.  The theme that doesn't come through in the base mechanics of the game is supplemented with the cards, which allow some wonderful uses of very specific concepts from the book without having to add special exceptions to the rules.  This game has its drawbacks as well. The rulebook is poorly organized, and figures are alarmingly difficult to distinguish, especially for the Free Peoples' forces.  Perhaps the biggest flaw, though, is how games seem to come down to the hunt tile draw in Mordor.  Drawing an eye tile in Mordor can be crippling, and drawing two is usually enough to end the game for the fellowship.  Although this heightens the drama on the last several turns, it also means the previous 2+ hours of the game were more or less irrelevant.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Excellent, satisfying, long fantasy wargame that I'd love to play more often.

RATING: 7
Extremely well-done recreation of the War for Middle Earth and the Quest of the Ringbearer(s). A military strategy game joined to a hunt-and-seek quest game, War of the Ring is very well balanced. Lots of great choices present themselves to either the Free Peoples or Shadow players. So many, in fact, that players may struggle to focus on getting one thing done well.

My only negative criticism of the game is that only 2 can play. The game system doesn't suggest any clean workarounds that would give additional players any significant share in making the critical decisions (well, any decision can be made by a committee instead of a solo-player, but I'm not sure that does much more than add confusion and complication - no real value add there).

RATING: 4
I cannot get the movie images out of my head, which is very unfortunate. But putting the fact that I am neither a Lord of the Ring nor, generally, a fantasy fan aside: Too many rules, too much fiddling with miniatures that don't fit into the territories, miniatures look far too similar (for someone who doesn't mess with spraypaint), takes a century and a half to set up. I am particularly not fond of rules like "If character A is in territory B and opponent's character C is not in play you may do XYZ in territory D except if card E has been played by opponent". It makes things very unpredictable and it's hard to develop any strategy, especially if you are the fellowship player and hence rely more on individual characters' cards. It is not entirely unenjoyable once you get into it a bit and it's got a nice board. I've been told it gets better once you know the game better - I guess I just don't like the idea of having to learn 20 cards by heart before being able to play the game properly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A brilliantly designed game which absolutely nails its theme. The shadow has endless hordes, while every free peoples soldier that is lost brings defeat by the hands of Sauron ever closer. The fellowship spends the game ducking and dodging the ring-wraiths that stalk them, as Frodo races against time to destroy the ring at Mount Doom. Everything about this game rings true to the Middle earth saga, which is a real credit to the design team.

It's also just dripping with plastic miniature goodness and every component is top notch. Unfortunately, there is one instance in which the designers worried more about aesthetics then functionality. The map, while providing a wonderfully detailed view of the political and cultural boundaries of Middle-Earth, is a train wreck when it comes to playing the game. The territories where most of the action takes place are far to small to hold the pieces involved, while outlying regions that never see play are huge. Another problem with the graphic design of the map is the cities, forts, and towns are all but indiscernible from one another. This can be a major annoyance, since each of these features has a different effect on battles that are conducted there, and thus it often requires constant referrals to the rulebook to determine under exactly what conditions you are actually fighting.

Another slight irritation stems from the miniatures, which are quite nice but all molded in the same color. Free peoples are blue, shadow nations are red. While I'm sure this was a production cost issue, the game requires you to track each nations participation in the War of the Ring separately. It's not too tough to differentiate between the shadow races since Orcs, Goblins and Easterlings are very easy to tell apart. The problem lies with the Free People armies, as the plastic miniatures of Rohan, Gondor and The North are almost identical in sculpt. Again, this causes extra, needless referrals to the pictures in the rulebook. Overall, it tends to unnecessarily complicate things in an already complicated game.

Nit picking aside, the asymmetrical gameplay and differing win conditions of the two sides are one of my favorite aspects of the game. It feels like each player is playing a different game, yet they intertwine flawlessly. I also enjoy the action dice mechanism. It adds quite a bit of interesting decision making to the game, although occasionally a bad roll can force you to essentially waste a turn. Still, the dice provide the perfect touch of chaos, so that you never know exactly what is going to happen on any given turn.

The end game is technically a little random, but nevertheless I enjoy the tension created as the last few tiles are drawn when Frodo makes his final approach to the Crack of Doom. A bigger concern is that the shadow side has more or less been solved. An aggressive move to the north can be very difficult for the Free People to stop, which can lead to an easy military victory for the shadow.

UPDATE: The Battles of the Third Age expansion has helped to take out some of the predictability that had started to creep into the game with repeated plays. If you already own this game, GET IT.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
I have the Collector's Edition--see my game comment there for my lengthy profession of love for this game.

Most of my games played have been with the expansion, but the base game is also great.  Some aspects are more scripted in the base game (kill Gandalf early as FP, attack Dale-Erebor-Woodland Realm as SP), and some are more scripted with the expansion (bring the Elves to war first as FP).  Overall, both have [i]plenty[/i] of strategic diversity though, so I wouldn't worry too much about it.

My only complaint is that the original board is a usability nightmare.  Despite the board being huge, many of the regions that see the most action are way too small, borders are wonky and hard to see (especially when packed with units), and settlements and cities are not nearly obvious enough.  The collector's edition board fixes all of these problems wonderfully, and hopefully they will use this same layout in the new revised edition.

Also, download the revised collector's edition rules, which are much easier to follow and reference.

RATING: 10
I just dont know about this one.  I think fiddly and underdeveloped comes to mind.  Like, very fiddly, and I usually dont mind fiddly games... Another issue that drives me to distraction is the serious component usability issues: Map areas are too small for the huge amount of minis, and the damn Nazgul are idiotically tall and prone to being knocked over.  I found this supremely annoying, and was highly distracted dealing with the miniatures, nazgul, and the disks that one uses to represent armies.  I found the components to be so annoying, that I had a hard time liking the game at all.  This game should have counters instead of miniatures.  

I liked the action dice mechanic, I didnt find it particularily limiting.  It made doing things to get more dice mandatory though, so things can get sort of scripted, at least in that regard.  To play this game even semi-competently, it is 100% necessary to know both players decks.  Strongly suggest reading all the cards before playing, otherwise frustration will set in.  Overall, meh.

Okay, the above was my ORIGINAL REVIEW.  I still don't like the fiddlyness of the pieces, but I've revised my opinion to calling this game a 10 after playing it more.  In fact, this might be the best game I've played in the last decade.

RATING: 10
What captures the feel of Tolkien's universe and condenses it into anywhere from 2 to 4 hours of nail-biting, fantastical action, where the Free Peoples hang by a thread as the armies of Sauron march across the landscape, pillaging and burning? If you said the Peter Jackson movie adaptation of the trilogy, then you'd be right! If you said War of The Ring, you'd also be right! I have yet to encounter a game wherein the universe/theme translates as well as this one does. Every game I play simply amazes me. The only problem I've ever had with this game is the totally disorganized rulebook, which can be realistically called an editor's nightmare. But if you take the time to learn WOTR from someone who knows how to play, then you will find it "goes down easy." 

If you ever see me at a Con and you WANT to learn how to play this incredible game, I'll gladly teach you.

RATING: 10
This is a beautiful and high quality game.  It is simple, plays fast, and has a high degree of player interaction which keeps all players in the game the whole time.  While it doesn't necessarily follow the books, this game manages to deliver an enjoyable narrative that could hardly be more evocative of Tolkien's world.  The game is fairly well balanced, and the expansion seems to further the balance.


-----
Added notes on the BotTA expansion to that game's page.  I would say ultimately that game balance is better without the expansion elements.  Better yet, just go with the second edition.  For posterity sake, I cannot see ever removing the "10" rating. Despite moving on to a new editions, it is still the same brilliant game.  I would consider this and 2e really a single "10" rating.

RATING: 3.5
A mish mosh of fairly good (production quality) and very bad (everything else). Very strong theme, excellent looking components (which don't fit on the board, by the way, and make identifying borders and regions nigh impossible). But the complex, arduous gameplay greatly detracts from the experience. I've never felt so uninvolved, bored and disinterested in my role in a game. Decisions are not very compelling, the exception-riddled rules are confounding, and the gameplay bogs down into a herky-jerky pace that really squeezes any enjoyment out of this. I never need (or want) to suffer through this again. I was delighted to trade this away for El Grande...

RATING: 9
Played at Origins.  First off, the look of the thing is fantastic.  Even with the pre-production copy we were playing you could sense that the final edition will just scream quality.  The pieces are FANTASTIC.  Those of you who thought that Quest for the Dragonlords was the shiznat will really get to see what good figures are.  The game itself is quite fun, and I look forward to playing it some more.  My buddy and I were evil, and we had a blast crushing the goodies.  It was a very close game.  I like the action dice as they sort of remind me of the card-driven wargames that I'm into right now.  Balancing the battle stuff against the various tracks and finding or moving the fellowship creates a nice bit of tension.  All-in-all seems like a must have.

UPDATE:  Holy god, the finished product and ruleset bumps this one up a notch. 10 I say, 10!

Only reason this is not a 10 is to let the Collector's Edition have a higher rating.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
1/22/09 -- A valiant effort, but it's hard to like a game that totally misinterprets its own inspiration.  The LOTR novels were so not about warfare, or its logistics, which is all this game seems to offer.  The entire drama of the Fellowship's adventures are relegated to an uninspired side track and the game is instead about how many orcs Sauruman can muster before the Ents turn up and other such trivialities.  I honestly feel like WotR is for the gaming equivalent of people who think Vivien Leigh should flash her tits in "Gone with the Wind", which is to say that it's for people who don't get it.

I also don't like the action dice.  I think their intent was to add a sense of uncertainty along the lines of the cards in a CDG, but it doesn't work.  The dice seem to mainly serve your opponents by letting them know what they can safely get away with each turn.  They were an interesting experiment, but I suppose the fact that we haven't seen them reused in other games since proves my point.

Still, it's hard not to like the game to some degree.  It is based on the LOTR saga after all, and events of the War of the Ring are interesting to see played out.  It's also not terribly long and it has flashy pieces.  I'll stand by a 7 rating for the time being.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I PLAYED IT (4 times so far). You need one game to get comfortable with the rules, but then it becomes a wonderful gaming experience, and you can set up and play the game in 3-4 hours. Important choices to be taken, tactics, atmosphere backed up with the best production ever - excellent miniatures AND extensive playtesting. I can still give this game +/- 1 point, but is currently my favourite.
